Proof from Causality (Sababiyya)
Sanusi argues from effect to cause, concluding the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from causality uses observable effects to prove the existence of a necessary Creator, and why chance cannot explain the universe's perfection.

Core Idea
Effects demand a cause; the universe's perfection demands a Creator.

Sanusi presents a simple yet profound argument: every effect points to its cause. Before we can accept that the universe came about by chance, we must examine the evidence. This passage lays out the logical link between the observable order of the cosmos and the existence of an intelligent Maker.

Guided Path
1
Trace the argument: effect to cause
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi begins with a general principle, then gives concrete examples. Underline the words for 'effect' (athar) and 'cause' (mu'aththir).
These terms recur throughout Islamic theology.
2
Connect the analogies to the universe
After reading the carpenter and shepherd analogies, see how Sanusi applies them to the heavens and the earth. Ask yourself: What does the sky and earth indicate about their Creator?
The Quranic verse emphasizes perfection.
3
Reflect on the refutation of chance
Consider the objection about chance. How does Sanusi argue that chance cannot produce the observed order? What is the logical flaw in attributing the universe to chance?
This refutation is central to the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - دليل السببية : ومعناه : أن الأثر يدل على المؤثر ، والسبب يدل على المسبب . أي أن وجود و الكر. مثلا : - وهو أثر - يدل على الإيمان بذات النجار : - أي المؤثر ـ الذي صنعه . وإن كنا لم نره . كما يقول الراعي : البعرة تدل على البعير ، والأثر يدل على المسير . فسماء ذات أبراج ، وأرض ذات فجاج . الا تدل على العليم الخبير. وإذا ما قال قائل : إن الدنيا مخلوقة بالمصادفة . نقول له : إن « الإحكام والإتقان ، في الكون يدل على الخالق ، ولا يدل على المصادفة . لأن المصادفة إذا أظهرت الإحكام صدفة في أمر من الأمور ، لا تظهره في كل الأمور . والحال أننا نرى العالم محكما ومتقنا صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء ) [النمل ۸۸] .
2 - The proof of causality: Its meaning is that the effect (athar) indicates the cause (mu'aththir), and the cause indicates the caused (musabbab). That is, the existence of ... for example—it is an effect—indicates belief in the carpenter—the cause—who made it, even if we have not seen him. As the shepherd says: The camel dung indicates the camel, and the footprint indicates the traveler. So a sky with constellations and an earth with valleys—does it not indicate the All-Knowing, All-Aware? And if someone says: The world is created by chance, we say to him: Precision and perfection in the universe indicate the Creator, and do not indicate chance. Because if chance produces precision accidentally in one thing, it does not produce it in all things. Yet we see the world precise and perfect—'the handiwork of God who perfected all things' [Quran 27:88].
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents the proof from causality (dalil al-sababiyya). It begins by stating the principle: the effect (athar) points to the cause (mu'aththir), and the cause points to the one who brings about the effect (musabbab). The author gives a simple analogy: seeing an effect like a chair implies a carpenter who made it, even if the carpenter is unseen. Similarly, a shepherd knows camel dung indicates a camel, and footprints indicate a traveler. Applying this to the universe, the sky with its constellations and the earth with its valleys clearly indicate an all-knowing and all-aware Creator. The passage then addresses an objection: what if someone claims the world came about by chance? The answer is that chance might produce order accidentally in one instance, but not consistently in all things. The universe, however, is uniformly precise and perfect, which is the handiwork of God. The proof concludes that the observed perfection necessitates an intelligent Creator, excluding chance.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab)
مسبب (musabbab)
أثر (athar)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
إحكام (ihkam)
إتقان (itqan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universe could have come about by random chance.
Correction: Chance cannot produce consistent order and precision throughout the universe; only an intelligent Creator can.

Checkpoint
What does the 'proof of causality' primarily argue against?
A) The existence of idols
B) The claim that the universe is eternal
C) The claim that the universe came about by chance
D) The existence of multiple gods

Key Takeaway
The observable order and perfection in the universe necessitate an intelligent Creator, ruling out chance as an explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky and the earth, do you genuinely see them as proofs of the Creator, or do you take them for granted? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized this argument?
?
If a friend told you that the order in your life is merely coincidence, how would you respond using the proof from causality? Would your answer reflect deep conviction or just memorized words?
?
The shepherd knew the camel from its dung. What small signs of Allah's handiwork have you overlooked today? Commit to noticing one such sign tomorrow and pausing to thank the Maker.

The Proof from Scientific Predictions in the Quran
Argues from Quranic scientific predictions to divine origin and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's scientific statements, unknown at the time of revelation and uttered by an unlettered prophet, constitute an independent proof of prophethood and thus of God's existence.

Core Idea
An unlettered prophet announcing hidden scientific facts proves divine origin.

The author presents a powerful argument: the Quran contains scientific knowledge that was totally unknown in 7th-century Arabia. Since the Prophet Muhammad was unlettered and had no teachers, this knowledge must have come from God. This lesson walks through that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage's core argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully. It sets up the two possibilities: either the Prophet learned these facts from a human teacher, or they were revealed by God.
Pay attention to the pivot word 'فإذن' (therefore).
2
Understand why a teacher is impossible
The author argues that no human teacher could have taught the Prophet these scientific facts because they were unknown to humanity for centuries after the Quran. This eliminates the first possibility.
The time gap of 14 centuries is key.
3
Connect to prophethood and God's existence
With the human teacher option removed, the only remaining source is God. Thus the Quran's divine origin proves Muhammad's prophethood, and through his truthfulness, we come to believe in God's existence.
This argument is independent and does not rely on other proofs.

٣ - دليل الآيات الكونية : ومعناه : أن نبي الإسلام محمدا نطق بآيات قرآنية تدل على امور علمية ، ما كان أحد يعرفها في زمانه . ونطق بها وهو أمي لا يقرأ ولا يكتب . ولم يكن دارسا ، ولم يلقنه معلم . فراعي غنم أمي وتاجر ينطق هذا الكلام العجيب الشأن يدل على أحد أمرين : إما أن معلما علمه ، وإما أن الله هو الذي علمه . لا جائز أن يكون المعلم . لأن الآيات العلمية لم يعرفها العلماء إلا بعد أربعة عشر قرنا من نزول القرآن . فإذن يكون مرسل الكلام - الذي هم القرآن - الله عز وجل - فإنه يؤمن الناس بوجود الله بدليل صدق محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فيها أخبر به ولقد رأيت بعض العلماء يفسر الآيات الكونية بأن كل شيء في هذا الكون هو أية ناطقة بأن الله ربها وخالقها . وقد سلك القرآن هذا السبيل لتقرير ربوبية الله والوهيته . ولذلك عندما نقرأ القرآن نراه يأخذنا في جولات وجولات يرتاد بنا آفاق السماء ، ويسير في جنبات الأرض ، ويقف بنا عند زهرات الحقول ، ويصعد بنا إلى النجوم في مداراتها ، وهو في كل ذلك يفتح أبصارنا وبصائرنا ، فيرينا كيف تعمل قدرة الله وتقديره في المخلوقات.. وهذا التفسير لا يصلح دليلا مستقلا عن دليل السببية - لأن هو نفسه دليل السببية - أي أنه إذا فسرنا الآيات الكونية بتفسيره هذا فمعناه أن الكون كله يدل على المسبب . ولكن إن فرناها دليلا على نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم من حيث نطقه بها في القرآن وهو أمي . فإنها تكون سببا مستقلا ، أي دليلا مستقلا
3 - The proof of cosmic signs: Its meaning is that the Prophet of Islam, Muhammad, uttered Quranic verses indicating scientific matters that no one knew in his time. He uttered them while he was unlettered, unable to read or write. He was not a student, nor was he taught by a teacher. An unlettered shepherd and trader pronouncing this wondrous speech points to one of two things: either a teacher taught him, or God taught him. It is not possible that it was a teacher, because the scientific verses were not known by scholars until fourteen centuries after the revelation of the Quran. Thus the sender of the speech—which is the Quran—is God Almighty, and people come to believe in the existence of God through the proof of the truthfulness of Muhammad (ﷺ) in what he reported. And I have seen some scholars interpret the cosmic signs as that everything in this universe is a speaking sign that God is its Lord and Creator. The Quran has taken this path to establish God's lordship and divinity. Therefore, when we read the Quran, we see it taking us on tour after tour, traversing the horizons of the sky, walking through the expanses of the earth, stopping with us at the flowers of the fields, ascending with us to the stars in their orbits, and in all of that opening our eyes and insights, showing us how God's power and decree work in creation. But this interpretation does not serve as an independent proof apart from the proof of causality—because it is itself the proof of causality—meaning that if we interpret the cosmic signs in this way, it implies that the entire universe indicates the Causer. However, if we make it a proof of the prophethood of Muhammad (ﷺ) from the aspect of his uttering them in the Quran while he was unlettered, then it becomes an independent proof, i.e., an independent evidence.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents two ways of understanding the proof from cosmic signs (dalil al-ayaat al-kawniyyah). The first way: the Quran contains scientific statements unknown at the time of revelation. The Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) was unlettered (ummi), so he could not have learned these from human teachers. Therefore, the source must be divine—God Himself. This proves the truthfulness of the Prophet, and through his truthfulness, the existence of God is established. The second interpretation, favored by some scholars, sees every natural phenomenon as a 'speaking sign' of God's lordship. The Quran itself uses this method by directing attention to the heavens, earth, and creatures. However, the author critiques this second interpretation: it is not independent from the proof of causality, because it simply states that the universe points to its Cause. But if the cosmic signs are used to prove prophethood (by highlighting that the unlettered Prophet mentioned them), then it becomes an independent proof. Thus the passage distinguishes between using the signs as a direct argument for God (which reduces to causality) and using them as evidence for prophethood (which is a distinct argument).

Key Terms

أمي
نبوة
السببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the argument from scientific miracles is weak because it relies on modern interpretations.
Correction: The author uses the fact that these statements were unknown at the time, not their modern confirmation. The argument is historical: the Prophet could not have known them, so the Quran must be from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible that a human teacher taught the Prophet the scientific facts?
A) Because the Prophet was too young
B) Because the scientific facts were not discovered until centuries after the Quran
C) Because the Prophet lived in a remote area
D) Because the facts are mentioned in previous scriptures

Key Takeaway
The Quran's scientific content, revealed to an unlettered prophet, provides an independent proof of its divine origin and Muhammad's prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly wrestled with the evidence for the Quran's divine origin, or have you just assumed it? What would it cost you to follow this proof to its conclusion—that Muhammad was indeed a prophet and that you must obey his message?
?
If the Quran's scientific predictions were unknown for 14 centuries and still point to God, what does that say about your own intellectual honesty when you encounter difficulties in faith? Are you ready to submit to the truth even if it demands change?
?
Consider your daily life: Are you living as if you truly believe the Quran is from God? What specific habit or thought pattern would change tonight if you acted on this proof with full conviction?

Cosmic Signs as Causality vs. Prophethood Proof
Distinguishes between cosmic signs as a direct proof for God and as a proof for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting every created thing as a sign of God is not an independent proof (it reduces to causality), but using it as evidence for prophethood (through the unlettered Prophet mentioning these signs) creates a distinct argument.

Core Idea
Everything as a sign of God is just causality; prophethood angle makes it a new proof.

The author now addresses an alternative understanding of 'cosmic signs'—that every natural phenomenon is a speaking sign of God's lordship. While this is a valid Quranic approach, the author explains that it does not constitute an independent proof beyond the standard argument from causality. However, he offers a way to make it independent: connect it to the Prophet's unlettered status.

Guided Path
1
Read about the alternative interpretation
Read from 'ولقد رأيت بعض العلماء' to the end. Notice how some scholars interpret cosmic signs as everything in the universe pointing to God.
This interpretation is common in Islamic theology.
2
Understand the critique
The author argues that this interpretation is not a separate proof because it simply states that the universe indicates its Cause—the same as the proof of causality (dalil al-sababiyya).
Causality is a fundamental principle in kalam.
3
See how it becomes independent
The author clarifies that if we use the cosmic signs to show that the unlettered Prophet mentioned them in the Quran, then it becomes an independent proof for prophethood, distinct from causality.
This is the key distinction: evidence for prophethood vs. evidence for God.

Proof from Guidance (Hidaya)
Contrasts fitrah with hidaya and presents two behavioral proofs for a Guide.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed guidance in human and animal behavior points to the existence of a Divine Guide, and how this proof differs from the proof of fitrah.

Core Idea
Guided behavior requires a Guide.

In this passage, the author presents a distinct argument for God's existence based on guidance (hidaya). Unlike the internal instinct of fitrah, hidaya is externally observed in the purposeful actions of humans and animals. By reflecting on who directs the intellect and instinct, we are led to acknowledge the Creator. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully, then the English translation. Notice the contrast the author draws between fitrah and hidaya right at the start.
The verse at the end is corrupted in the source; the correct Quranic text is 'ثم هدى' (then guided).
2
Contrast fitrah and hidaya
Identify the key differences: fitrah is internal and instinctive, emerging upon stimulation; hidaya is observed in external behavior and actions. Understand why this distinction matters for the argument.
3
Analyze the human behavior argument
The passage asks: who guided the intellect to discern beneficial from harmful actions? The answer points to Allah. Reflect on how this reasoning works.
4
Analyze the animal behavior argument
Animals perform complex functions without being taught. This indicates they are guided by their Creator. Note how the verse from Surah Taha supports this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - دليل الهداية : والفرق بين هذا الدليل ودليل القطرة : أن الفطرة غريزة كامنة عند الإنسان تظهر حال الاستثارة أو الاستفزاز ويحس بها الإنسان نفسه. وأما الهداية فمعناها : النظر إلى تصرف الإنسان وتصرف الحيوان الأعجم. إن تصرف الإنسان يدل على وجود العقل فيه . فمن الذي هدى العقل إلى أن هذا الفعل مفيد أو غير مفيد ؟ إنه الله عز وجل فتصرف الحيوان يدل على أن الله هدى المخلوقات لكي تقوم بالوظائف التي خلقها الله من أجلها ، وقيامها بالوظائف يدل على أن لهذه المخلوقات خالقا . ولذلك قال تعالى : ( ربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ، ثم مدي ) [طه ٥٠] .
4 - The proof of guidance: The difference between this proof and the proof of fitrah (innate disposition) is that fitrah is an innate instinct latent in man, which appears upon stimulation or provocation, and man himself senses it. As for guidance (hidaya), its meaning is: looking at the behavior of man and the behavior of the dumb animal. The behavior of man indicates the existence of intellect in him. So who guided the intellect to know that this action is beneficial or not? It is God Almighty. The behavior of the animal indicates that God guided the creatures to perform the functions for which God created them, and their performance of these functions indicates that these creatures have a Creator. Therefore He said, the Exalted: 'Our Lord is He who gave each thing its form and then guided [it]' [Quran 20:50].
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage explains the proof from guidance (dalil al-hidaya) and distinguishes it from the proof from fitrah. Fitrah is an internal, instinctive awareness that emerges upon stimulation. In contrast, hidaya focuses on external observation of behavior—both human and animal. Human behavior demonstrates the presence of intellect ('aql); the question is: who guided the intellect to discern beneficial from harmful actions? The answer is God. Similarly, animals instinctively perform complex functions (e.g., building nests, migrating) without being taught. This implies that God has guided them. Since they fulfill their created purposes, this necessitates a Creator who designed and guided them. The Quranic verse concludes the argument: God gives each creature its specific nature and then guides it accordingly. Thus, the guidance inherent in creation points back to the Guide, God.

Key Terms
هداية (hidaya)
فطرة (fitrah)
العقل (al-'aql)
تصرف (tasarruf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the proof of fitrah and the proof of hidaya are the same.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes them: fitrah is an internal instinct, while hidaya is externally observed guidance in behavior.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between the proof of fitrah and the proof of hidaya?
A) Fitrah is based on emotion, hidaya on intellect
B) Fitrah is internal and instinctive; hidaya is observed in behavior
C) Fitrah is for humans only, hidaya includes animals
D) Fitrah is a Quranic proof, hidaya is a rational proof

Key Takeaway
The guidance observed in human and animal behavior logically requires an Intelligent Guide, which is Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you consciously seek Allah's guidance, or do you rely solely on your own intellect? How might your choices change if you truly believed your intellect is dependent on Allah's direction?
?
Observe the behavior of an animal—perhaps a bird building a nest or a bee making honey. Does this sight remind you that Allah guides even the smallest creatures? How does this awareness affect your trust in His guidance for your own life?
?
If the guidance of your intellect is from Allah, then when you disobey Him, you are using His gift against Him. How does this realization make you feel? What will you do today to better align your actions with His guidance?

The Proof of Creation: The Cosmological Argument
Al-Aqqad introduces the four proofs and elaborates on the first: the proof of creation (cosmological argument).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Aqqad's cosmological argument for God's existence, including the concept of the unmoved mover and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The universe's contingency points directly to a necessary Creator.

Al-Aqqad begins by presenting what he considers the oldest, simplest, and strongest rational proof: the proof of creation. This argument, known in Western philosophy as the cosmological argument, establishes that all contingent beings require an external cause, ultimately leading to a necessary being—God. Read the passage to grasp the core reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to familiarize yourself with the argument.
2
Identify the main premises
Notice that al-Aqqad argues that existents depend on others and cannot be self-sufficient. The chain must end in a self-existent cause.
3
Understand the 'unmoved mover'
The proof culminates in a mover that does not move—God. Consider how this avoids infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the key objection
Al-Aqqad rejects the idea that the sum of imperfections can produce perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول الأستاذ عباس محمود العقاد في : الله : : إن أقرب الأدلة العقلية إلى القبول على وجود الله تعالى هو : برهان الخلق ، وبرهان الغاية ، وبرهان الاستكمال - أي المثل العليا - وبرهان الاخلاق . 1 - أما برهان الخلق : ويعرف في اللغات الأوروبية باسم البرهان الكوني . فهو أقدم هذه البراهين وأبسطها وأقواها في اعتقادنا على الإقناع. وخلاصته : إن الموجودات لا بد لها من موجد لأننا نرى كل موجود منها يتوقف على غيره ، ونرى غيره هذا يتوقف على موجود آخر ، دون أن نعرف ضرورة توجب وجوده لذاته، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الموجودات كلها ناقصة ، وأن الكمال يتحقق في الكون كله . لأن هذا كالقول بأن مجموع النقص : كمال . ومجموع المتناهيات : شيء ليس له انتهاء . ومجموع القصور : قدرة لا يعتبر بها المقصور. فإذا كانت الموجودات غير واجبة لذاتها ، فلا بد لها من سبب يوجبها ، ولا يتوقف وجوده على وجود سبب سواء . ويسمى هذا البرهان - في أسلوب من أساليبه المتعددة - ببرهان المحرك الذي لا يتحرك ، أو المحرك الذي انشا جميع الحركات الكونية على اختلاف معانيها . ومنها الحركة بمعنى الانتقال من حال إلى حال . والحركة بمعنى الانتقال من حيز الإمكان إلى حيز الوجود، أو من حيز القوة إلى حيز الفعل . وفحوى البرهان : أن المحرك لا بد له من محرك ، وأن هذا المحرك لا بد وأن يستمد الحركة من غيره . وهكذا إلى أن يقف العقل عند محرك واحد ، لا تجوز عليه الحركة ، لأنه قائم بغير حدود من المكان أو الزمان . وهذا هو الله [ ٢ - وأما برهان الغاية : فهو في لبابه نمط موسع من برهان الخلق ، مع تصرف فيه وزيادة عليه . لأنه يتخذ من المخلوقات دليلا على وجود الخلق ، ويزيد على ذلك : أن هذه المخلوقات تدل على قصد في تكوينها ، وحكمة في تسيررها وتدبيرها . ٣ - وأما برهان الاستكمال : ويسمى برهان المثل العليا : وفحواه : أن العقل الإنساني كلما تصور شيئا عظيما، تصور ما هو أعظم منه . لأن الوقوف بالعظمة عند مرتبة قاصرة يحتاج إلى. وهو - أي العقل الإنساني - لا يعرف سبب القصور . فيا من شيء كامل ، إلا والعقل الإنساني متطلع إلى أكمل منه ، ثم أكمل منه ، إلى نهاية النهايات ، وهي غاية الكمال المطلق التي لا مزيد عليها ولا نقص فيها . وهذا الموجود الكامل الذي لا مزيد على كماله : موجود لا محالة . لأن وجوده في التصور أقل من وجوده في الحقيقة ، فهو في الحقيقة : موجود. لأن الكمال المطلق ينتفي عنه ، بسبب عدم وجوده ، ولا يبقى له شيء من الكمال ، بل نقص مطلق هو عدم الوجود ، فمجرد تصور الكمال مثبت لوجوده هذا ٤ - وأما برهان الأخلاق : فهو علامة ، في النفس الإنسانية. لا يتأتي وجودها فيها وجود إله . وتلك هي علامة الوازع الأخلاقي، أو علامة الواجب أو علامة الضمير . فمن أين استوجب
And Professor Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad says in [his book] 'God': The closest rational proofs to acceptance for the existence of God Almighty are: the proof of creation, the proof of purpose, the proof of completion (that is, the ideal archetypes), and the proof of morality. 1 - As for the proof of creation: It is known in European languages as the cosmological argument. It is the oldest of these proofs, the simplest, and in our belief the strongest in persuasion. Its summary: Existents must have a Creator because we see every existent depends on another, and we see that other depends on another existent, without knowing a necessity that requires its existence by itself. And it cannot be said that all existents are deficient and that perfection is realized in the entire universe, because this is like saying that the sum of deficiency is perfection, and the sum of finite things is something without end, and the sum of incapacity is power that is not regarded with limitation. So if existents are not necessary by themselves, they must have a cause that necessitates them, and whose existence does not depend on the existence of any cause. This proof—in one of its multiple forms—is called the proof of the unmoved mover, or the mover that originated all cosmic movements in their various meanings. Among them is movement in the sense of transition from one state to another, and movement in the sense of transition from the realm of possibility to the realm of existence, or from the realm of potentiality to the realm of actuality. The gist of the proof: every mover must have a mover, and that mover must derive movement from another, and so on until the mind stops at one mover upon whom movement is not possible, because He is established without limits of place or time. This is God. [ 2 - As for the proof of purpose: It is in essence an expanded pattern of the proof of creation, with modification and addition. Because it takes the creatures as evidence for the existence of the Creator, and adds to that: that these creatures indicate intention in their formation, and wisdom in their management and administration. 3 - As for the proof of completion: It is called the proof of the ideal archetypes. Its gist: The human intellect, whenever it conceives of something great, conceives of something greater than it. Because stopping at greatness at a limited level requires... And it—the human intellect—does not know the reason for the limitation. So there is no perfect thing except that the human intellect aspires to something more perfect, then more perfect, until the end of ends, which is the ultimate absolute perfection that has no increase and no deficiency. And this perfect existent that has no increase upon its perfection necessarily exists. Because its existence in conception is less than its existence in reality, so in reality it exists. Because absolute perfection would be negated due to its non-existence, and nothing of perfection would remain, but only absolute deficiency which is non-existence. So merely conceiving of perfection affirms its existence. This [is the proof]. 4 - As for the proof of morality: It is a sign in the human soul. Its existence in it does not occur without the existence of God. And that is the sign of the moral deterrent, or the sign of duty, or the sign of conscience. So from where does it require...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents four rational proofs for God's existence as articulated by Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad. The first proof (burhan al-khalq) is the cosmological argument: all contingent beings require a cause; an infinite regress is impossible, so there must be a necessary being (the unmoved mover). The author elaborates on the impossibility of perfection arising from a collection of imperfections, and explains that the chain of movers terminates in a mover that does not move—God. The second proof (burhan al-ghaya) is the teleological argument: creatures exhibit design and purpose, pointing to an intelligent Creator. The third proof (burhan al-istikmal) is the argument from degrees of perfection: the human mind can always conceive of something greater, leading to the concept of absolute perfection, which must exist in reality, not merely in conception. The fourth proof (burhan al-akhlaq) is the moral argument: the presence of a moral sense (conscience) in humans cannot be explained without a divine source. The passage ends abruptly, but the moral argument is cut off.

Key Terms
برهان الخلق (burhan al-khalq)
المحرك الذي لا يتحرك (al-muharrik alladhi la yataharrak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of creation is the same as the ontological argument (based on definition).
Correction: Al-Aqqad's proof is cosmological: it argues from contingency and motion, not from the definition of God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Aqqad, which proof is considered the oldest, simplest, and strongest?
A) Proof of purpose
B) Proof of creation
C) Proof of completion
D) Proof of morality

Key Takeaway
Everything contingent requires a cause, and an infinite chain of causes is impossible, leading to a single uncaused cause—God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the objects around you—your phone, your body, the stars—do you truly see them as contingent, hanging by a thread from the Creator? Or do you live as if they exist on their own? What would change in your daily gratitude if you internalized this dependency?
?
Al-Aqqad says the sum of imperfections cannot become perfection. Yet you often try to patch your spiritual shortcomings by adding more good deeds, more knowledge, more efforts—as if piling up finite things can make you infinite. Where in your life are you trusting in accumulation rather than turning to the perfect One? Will you repent from that self-sufficiency today?
?
The proof ends with a Mover who does not move—God outside time and space. But you live as if you are the mover of your own life. In what area are you acting as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your very existence is caused by Him every moment? What step will you take tonight to acknowledge your absolute need for Him?

The Proof of Purpose: The Teleological Argument
Al-Aqqad presents the teleological argument as the second proof for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Aqqad's teleological argument for God's existence, which points to design and purpose in creation.

Core Idea
Order and purpose in the universe require a deliberate designer.

The second proof builds upon the first by adding the observation that creatures not only exist but exhibit purpose and wisdom. Al-Aqqad calls this an expanded version of the creation argument. In this lesson, you will see how teleology reinforces the case for a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation to understand the essence of the proof of purpose.
2
Compare with the first proof
Al-Aqqad describes this proof as an expanded pattern of the creation argument. Note the additional element: intention (qasd) and wisdom (hikma).
3
Reflect on examples
Think of natural phenomena that exhibit clear purpose, such as the human eye or the water cycle.

The Proof of Completion: The Argument from Degrees of Perfection
Al-Aqqad presents the argument from degrees of perfection, also called the proof of ideal archetypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Aqqad's argument from degrees of perfection, which posits that the human mind's conception of absolute perfection necessitates its existence in reality.

Core Idea
The mind's endless pursuit of perfection points to a truly perfect being that must exist.

The third proof, called the proof of completion (burhan al-istikmal) or the proof of ideal archetypes, is based on a psychological observation: whenever we conceive of something great, we can conceive of something greater. This ascent leads to the idea of absolute perfection, which must exist because otherwise it would not be truly perfect. In this lesson, you will explore this intriguing argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the psychological premise
Al-Aqqad says the intellect always conceives of something greater than any given greatness. This is key.
3
Understand the conclusion
If absolute perfection were only a concept, it would lack existence, which is a deficiency. Therefore, it must exist in reality.
4
Compare to other proofs
Notice how this proof differs from the first two: it starts from within the mind, not from external observation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The human mind's unending pursuit of greater perfection logically requires the actual existence of absolute perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Proof of Morality: The Moral Argument
Al-Aqqad presents the moral argument as the fourth proof, though the passage is incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Aqqad's moral argument for God's existence, which grounds the sense of duty and conscience in a divine source.

Core Idea
Our innate moral sense requires a divine origin beyond human convention.

The fourth and final proof presented by al-Aqqad is the moral argument. He describes it as a 'sign' in the human soul—a sign that cannot exist without God. This sign is the moral deterrent (al-wazi' al-akhlaqi), the sense of duty, or conscience. The passage is incomplete, but the core idea is clear: morality points to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation. Note that the passage cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the key concept
Al-Aqqad refers to the 'moral deterrent' or 'conscience' as a sign of God.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why would a sense of objective moral duty exist in us if there is no supreme lawgiver? This is the core of the argument.

ويقول الأستاذ عباس محمود العقاد في : الله : : إن أقرب الأدلة العقلية إلى القبول على وجود الله تعالى هو : برهان الخلق ، وبرهان الغاية ، وبرهان الاستكمال - أي المثل العليا - وبرهان الاخلاق . 1 - أما برهان الخلق : ويعرف في اللغات الأوروبية باسم البرهان الكوني . فهو أقدم هذه البراهين وأبسطها وأقواها في اعتقادنا على الإقناع. وخلاصته : إن الموجودات لا بد لها من موجد لأننا نرى كل موجود منها يتوقف على غيره ، ونرى غيره هذا يتوقف على موجود آخر ، دون أن نعرف ضرورة توجب وجوده لذاته، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الموجودات كلها ناقصة ، وأن الكمال يتحقق في الكون كله . لأن هذا كالقول بأن مجموع النقص : كمال . ومجموع المتناهيات : شيء ليس له انتهاء . ومجموع القصور : قدرة لا يعتبر بها المقصور. فإذا كانت الموجودات غير واجبة لذاتها ، فلا بد لها من سبب يوجبها ، ولا يتوقف وجوده على وجود سبب سواء . ويسمى هذا البرهان - And Professor Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad says in [his book] 'God': The closest rational proofs to acceptance for the existence of God Almighty are: the proof of creation, the proof of purpose, the proof of completion (that is, the ideal archetypes), and the proof of morality. 1 - As for the proof of creation: It is known in European languages as the cosmological argument. It is the oldest of these proofs, the simplest, and in our belief the strongest in persuasion. Its summary: Existents must have a Creator because we see every existent depends on another, and we see that other depends on another existent, without knowing a necessity that requires its existence by itself. And it cannot be said that all existents are deficient and that perfection is realized in the entire universe, because this is like saying that the sum of deficiency is perfection, and the sum of finite things is something without end, and the sum of incapacity is power that is not regarded with limitation. So if existents are not necessary by themselves, they must have a cause that necessitates them, and whose existence does not depend on the existence of any cause. This proof—in one of its multiple forms—is called the proof of the unmoved mover, or the mover that originated all cosmic movements in their various meanings. Among them is movement in the sense of transition from one state to another, and movement in the sense of transition from the realm of possibility to the realm of existence, or from the realm of potentiality to the realm of actuality. The gist of the proof: every mover must have a mover, and that mover must derive movement from another, and so on until the mind stops at one mover upon whom movement is not possible, because He is established without limits of place or time. This is God. [ 2 - As for the proof of purpose: It is in essence an expanded pattern of the proof of creation, with modification and addition. Because it takes the creatures as evidence for the existence of the Creator, and adds to that: that these creatures indicate intention in their formation, and wisdom in their management and administration. 3 - As for the proof of completion: It is called the proof of the ideal archetypes. Its gist: The human intellect, whenever it conceives of something great, conceives of something greater than it. Because stopping at greatness at a limited level requires... And it—the human intellect—does not know the reason for the limitation. So there is no perfect thing except that the human intellect aspires to something more perfect, then more perfect, until the end of ends, which is the ultimate absolute perfection that has no increase and no deficiency. And this perfect existent that has no increase upon its perfection necessarily exists. Because its existence in conception is less than its existence in reality, so in reality it exists. Because absolute perfection would be negated due to its non-existence, and nothing of perfection would remain, but only absolute deficiency which is non-existence. So merely conceiving of perfection affirms its existence. This [is the proof]. 4 - As for the proof of morality: It is a sign in the human soul. Its existence in it does not occur without the existence of God. And that is the sign of the moral deterrent, or the sign of duty, or the sign of conscience. So from where does it require...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents four rational proofs for God's existence as articulated by Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad. The first proof (burhan al-khalq) is the cosmological argument: all contingent beings require a cause; an infinite regress is impossible, so there must be a necessary being (the unmoved mover). The author elaborates on the impossibility of perfection arising from a collection of imperfections, and explains that the chain of movers terminates in a mover that does not move—God. The second proof (burhan al-ghaya) is the teleological argument: creatures exhibit design and purpose, pointing to an intelligent Creator. The third proof (burhan al-istikmal) is the argument from degrees of perfection: the human mind can always conceive of something greater, leading to the concept of absolute perfection, which must exist in reality, not merely in conception. The fourth proof (burhan al-akhlaq) is the moral argument: the presence of a moral sense (conscience) in humans cannot be explained without a divine source. The passage ends abruptly, but the moral argument is cut off.

Key Terms
برهان الأخلاق (burhan al-akhlaq)
الوازع الأخلاقي (al-wazi' al-akhlaqi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moral argument is the same as the argument from design.
Correction: The moral argument is distinct: it focuses on inner conscience and duty, not external order.

Key Takeaway
The existence of conscience and moral duty in humans is a sign that points to the existence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Rational Proofs for God's Existence
Al-Aqqad enumerates four rational proofs with a Quranic citation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational proofs for God's existence as enumerated by Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad and the Quranic foundation for creation.

Core Idea
Four rational proofs point to the same Creator: creation, purpose, perfection, and morality.

Al-Aqqad begins his discussion by grounding reason in revelation: the Quranic verse that every creature has a Creator who guides it. He then lists four rational proofs that are most readily accepted by the human mind. This lesson introduces each proof and sets the stage for deeper exploration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic verse citation and the list format.
2
Identify the Quranic verse
Find the verse from Surah Ta-Ha and understand its role: it affirms that every creation has a Creator and guidance.
This verse provides a scriptural foundation for the rational arguments.
3
Recognize the four proofs
The passage explicitly names four proofs: creation, purpose, perfection (ideal forms), and morality. Note the Arabic terms for each.
These correspond to cosmological, teleological, and moral arguments in philosophy.
4
Reflect on 'closest to acceptance'
Al-Aqqad claims these are the most acceptable rational proofs. Think about why each might resonate with human reason.
They are complementary, not conflicting.
5
Preview the coming lessons
Each proof will be explained in detail. This lesson is the overview. Write down any questions you have about each proof.

المخلوقات خالقا . ولذلك قال تعالى : ( ربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ، ثم هدى ) [طه ٥٠] . (ب) ويقول الأستاذ عباس محمود العقاد في : الله : : إن أقرب الأدلة العقلية إلى القبول على وجود الله تعالى هو : برهان الخلق ، وبرهان الغاية ، وبرهان الاستكمال - أي المثل العليا - وبرهان الاخلاق .

(B) And Professor Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad says in his book 'Allah': 'Indeed, the closest rational proofs to acceptance regarding the existence of Allah the Most High are: the proof of creation, the proof of purpose, the proof of perfection - i.e., the ideal forms - and the proof of morality.'
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage begins by citing a Quranic verse affirming that every creature has a Creator who also guides it. It then introduces the modern Egyptian thinker Abbas Mahmoud al-Aqqad, who in his book 'Allah' enumerates four rational arguments for the existence of God. These are: (1) the proof of creation (burhan al-khalq), which is the cosmological argument; (2) the proof of purpose (burhan al-ghaya), the teleological argument; (3) the proof of perfection (burhan al-istikmal), also called the argument from ideal forms; and (4) the proof of morality (burhan al-akhlaq), the moral argument. This enumeration sets the framework for the detailed exposition of each argument that follows.

Misconception: These four proofs are independent and each alone suffices as absolute proof.
Correction: While each is compelling, they are complementary and together provide a cumulative case for God's existence.

The Contingency Argument
Al-Aqqad explains the contingency form of the cosmological proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contingency argument as the core of the cosmological proof and why a self-necessary cause is required.

Core Idea
Everything contingent depends on a Necessary Being.

Al-Aqqad begins with the oldest and simplest proof for God's existence: the cosmological argument. He observes that every existing thing depends on something else, and argues that the universe as a whole cannot be self-sufficient. In this lesson, we explore the argument from contingency and its refutation of the idea that the whole is perfect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the proof starts with the observation that every existent depends on another. This is the key premise.
3
Understand the objection
Al-Aqqad considers the objection that the universe as a whole might be self-sufficient. Find that objection in the text.
4
Grasp the refutation
The refutation uses an analogy: a collection of deficiencies cannot equal perfection. See how this logic works.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that there must be a cause that is self-necessary. This sets up the unmoved mover argument.
This lesson covers only the contingency part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 - أما برهان الخلق : ويعرف في اللغات الأوروبية باسم البرهان الكوني . فهو أقدم هذه البراهين وأبسطها وأقواها في اعتقادنا على الإقناع. وخلاصته : إن الموجودات لا بد لها من موجد لأننا نرى كل موجود منها يتوقف على غيره ، ونرى غيره هذا يتوقف على موجود آخر ، دون أن نعرف ضرورة توجب وجوده لذاته، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الموجودات كلها ناقصة ، وأن الكمال يتحقق في الكون كله . لأن هذا كالقول بأن مجموع النقص : كمال . ومجموع المتناهيات : شيء ليس له انتهاء . ومجموع القصور : قدرة لا يعتبر بها المقصور. فإذا كانت الموجودات غير واجبة لذاتها ، فلا بد لها من سبب يوجبها ، ولا يتوقف وجوده على وجود سبب سواء . ويسمى هذا البرهان - في أسلوب من أساليبه المتعددة - ببرهان المحرك الذي لا يتحرك ، أو المحرك الذي أنشأ جميع الحركات الكونية على اختلاف معانيها . ومنها الحركة بمعنى الانتقال من حال إلى حال . والحركة بمعنى الانتقال من حيز الإمكان إلى حيز الوجود، أو من حيز القوة إلى حيز الفعل . وفحوى البرهان : أن المحرك لا بد له من محرك ، وأن هذا المحرك لا بد وأن يستمد الحركة من غيره . وهكذا إلى أن يقف العقل عند محرك واحد ، لا تجوز عليه الحركة ، لأنه قائم بغير حدود من المكان أو الزمان . وهذا هو الله
1. As for the proof of creation: It is known in European languages as the cosmological proof. It is the oldest of these proofs, the simplest, and in our belief the most convincing. Its summary: that existing things must have a creator, because we see every existing thing depending on another, and we see that other depending on another existent, without our knowing a necessity obligating its existence by itself. And it cannot be said that all existing things are deficient and that perfection is realized in the whole universe, because this is like saying the sum of deficiency is perfection, and the sum of finites is something without end, and the sum of shortcomings is a power not considered by the one who is deficient. So if existing things are not necessary by themselves, they must have a cause that necessitates them, and whose existence does not depend on the existence of any cause. This proof is called - in one of its multiple forms - the proof of the unmoved mover, or the mover who originated all cosmic motions in their different meanings. Among them is motion in the sense of transition from state to state, and motion in the sense of transition from the domain of possibility to the domain of existence, or from the domain of potentiality to the domain of actuality. The core of the proof is that the mover must have a mover, and that this mover must derive motion from something else, and so on until the mind stops at a single mover for whom motion is not possible, because He is established without limits of space or time. And this is Allah.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the cosmological argument (burhan al-khalq) in detail. It begins by noting that the argument is known as the cosmological proof in European languages and states that it is the oldest, simplest, and most convincing of the four proofs. The argument proceeds from the observation that every existent thing depends on something else; nothing we see exists by its own essence. Some might claim that the universe as a whole is self-sufficient, but the author refutes this by analogy: a collection of deficiencies cannot produce perfection, a collection of finites cannot be infinite, and a collection of shortcomings cannot amount to power. Since individual things are not self-necessary, they require an external cause that is itself necessary and not dependent on any other cause. The proof is then reformulated as the argument from motion, where every motion (including change from potentiality to actuality) requires a mover, and this chain cannot regress infinitely. It must terminate in a First Mover who is not subject to motion, transcending space and time. That First Mover is identified as Allah.

Key Terms
برهان الخلق
البرهان الكوني
موجد
سبب
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cosmological proof is based on the observation of order and design in the universe.
Correction: Al-Aqqad's proof is based on the contingency and dependence of beings, not on design. It argues that nothing in the universe is self-sufficient, regardless of order.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the flaw in the objection that the universe as a whole is perfect?
A) The universe is not perfect
B) A collection of deficiencies cannot equal perfection
C) The universe is necessary by itself
D) The objection is not considered

Key Takeaway
Individual contingent beings point to a necessary cause that does not depend on anything else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a collection of deficiencies cannot become perfection. In what areas of your life are you trusting in your own accumulations—knowledge, deeds, wealth—to make you whole? Will you turn to the only necessary cause today?
?
You acknowledge that everything depends on Allah, but does your daily worry reveal a practical belief in self-sufficiency? What would change if you truly lived as though your very breath depends on the Necessary Being?
?
The refutation destroys the excuse that 'the whole is perfect.' When you cover your sins with good deeds or excuses, are you not making the same claim over your own soul? How will you stop treating your deficiencies as if they add up to righteousness?

The Unmoved Mover Argument
Al-Aqqad presents the unmoved mover version of the cosmological proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from motion (unmoved mover) as a form of the cosmological proof.

Core Idea
Every motion requires a First Mover beyond space and time.

Al-Aqqad now presents the cosmological proof in its form as the argument from motion. He defines motion broadly as any transition—from state to state, from potentiality to actuality. By tracing the chain of movers, we are led to a single mover who is not subject to motion. This is the unmoved mover, and it is Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the definition of motion.
2
Define the types of motion
Al-Aqqad lists three meanings of motion. Identify them and understand how they differ.
3
Trace the chain of movers
The core is that every mover is moved by another. Follow the reasoning to its logical terminus.
4
Understand the conclusion
The chain must stop at a mover who is not subject to motion, transcending space and time. This is the First Mover.
5
Reflect on the implication
The First Mover is identified as Allah. Consider why this conclusion is necessary.

This passage presents the cosmological argument (burhan al-khalq) in detail. It begins by noting that the argument is known as the cosmological proof in European languages and states that it is the oldest, simplest, and most convincing of the four proofs. The argument proceeds from the observation that every existent thing depends on something else; nothing we see exists by its own essence. Some might claim that the universe as a whole is self-sufficient, but the author refutes this by analogy: a collection of deficiencies cannot produce perfection, a collection of finites cannot be infinite, and a collection of shortcomings cannot amount to power. Since individual things are not self-necessary, they require an external cause that is itself necessary and not dependent on any other cause. The proof is then reformulated as the argument from motion, where every motion (including change from potentiality to actuality) requires a mover, and this chain cannot regress infinitely. It must terminate in a First Mover who is not subject to motion, transcending space and time. That First Mover is identified as Allah.

Key Terms
برهان المحرك الذي لا يتحرك
الحركة
الإمكان
الفعل
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from motion only applies to local motion (movement in space).
Correction: Al-Aqqad defines motion broadly as any change, including transition from possibility to actuality and from state to state. It covers all change.

Checkpoint
What is the core conclusion of the unmoved mover argument?
A) There must be an infinite chain of movers
B) Motion is eternal
C) There must be a mover that does not itself move
D) The universe is self-moving

Key Takeaway
The chain of motion leads to a First Mover who transcends space, time, and change — that is Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe constant change in your own life—your moods, your desires, your circumstances—does it unsettle you, or do you anchor yourself in the Unmoved Mover who never changes? What would change if you truly lived with that anchor?
?
If the First Mover is beyond space and time, how do you reconcile that with your tendency to imagine Allah as distant or indifferent? Are you willing to let go of mental images that limit Him?
?
The proof shows that every reason for motion points back to a single Source. Are you seeking ultimate stability in anything that moves and changes—people, possessions, status? Will you commit today to trace every security back to Allah alone?

The Teleological Proof: From Creation to Design
Al-Aqqad defines the teleological proof and its relationship to the cosmological proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the teleological proof expands upon the cosmological argument by highlighting purpose and wisdom in creation.

Core Idea
Design in creation points to an intelligent Designer.

Al-Aqqad introduces the teleological proof as a deeper version of the cosmological argument. While the cosmological proof reasons from existence to a Creator, the teleological proof goes further by seeing purpose and wisdom in the universe. This short passage distinguishes the two proofs and shows how the teleological argument strengthens the case for a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

Notice that the passage begins with a statement about the teleological proof being an expanded pattern.
2

The passage explicitly says the teleological proof adds purpose and wisdom.
3
Compare with the cosmological proof
Think about how the teleological proof builds on the cosmological proof. The cosmological proof argues from existence, while the teleological proof argues from design and purpose.
Al-Aqqad calls it an 'expanded pattern' meaning it includes the cosmological proof and adds more.

This additional observation strengthens the argument by showing that the Creator is not just a necessary cause but an intelligent designer who acts with intention.

The Argument from Perfection (Burhan al-Istikmal)
Al-Aqqad explains the argument from perfection, also known as the argument from ideal forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from perfection demonstrates that absolute perfection must exist in reality.

Core Idea
The very concept of perfection demands a real perfect being.

This lesson presents the so-called 'argument from perfection' (burhan al-istikmal), also known as the argument from ideal forms. It begins with the observation that the human mind always seeks a greater perfection beyond any limited perfection. From this, Al-Aqqad argues that the idea of absolute perfection points to a really existent perfect being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical style.
2
Identify the core observation
The argument starts with a psychological observation: the human mind, whenever it conceives something great, conceives something greater. Note that this is presented as a universal fact.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace how the argument moves from limited perfections to an infinite regress, and then to the necessity of a terminating absolute perfection.
The key step is that the mind cannot stop at a deficient level without a cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the absolutely perfect being must exist in reality, because existence in reality is more perfect than existence in conception alone.
5
Reflect on the uniqueness of this argument
Consider why this argument does not claim that every conceived thing exists. The key is that absolute perfection uniquely entails existence because non-existence would imply deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ - وأما برهان الاستكمال : ويسمى برهان المثل العليا : وفحواه : أن العقل الإنساني كلما تصور شيئا عظيما، تصور ما هو أعظم منه . لأن الوقوف بالعظمة عند مرتبة قاصرة يحتاج إلى. وهو - أي العقل الإنساني - لا يعرف سبب القصور . فما من شيء كامل ، إلا والعقل الإنساني متطلع إلى أكمل منه ، ثم أكمل منه ، إلى نهاية النهايات ، وهي غاية الكمال المطلق التي لا مزيد عليها ولا نقص فيها . وهذا الموجود الكامل الذي لا مزيد على كماله : موجود لا محالة . لأن وجوده في التصور أقل من وجوده في الحقيقة ، فهو في الحقيقة : موجود. لأن الكمال المطلق ينتفي عنه ، بسبب عدم وجوده ، ولا يبقى له شيء من الكمال ، بل نقص مطلق هو عدم الوجود ، فمجرد تصور الكمال مثبت لوجوده
3. As for the proof of perfection: it is called the proof of ideal forms. Its core: that the human mind, whenever it conceives of something great, conceives of something greater than it. Because stopping at greatness at a deficient level requires [a cause]. And the human mind does not know the cause of the deficiency. There is no perfect thing except that the human mind is aspiring to something more perfect than it, then more perfect than it, until the end of ends, which is the ultimate absolute perfection that has no increase and no deficiency. And this perfect being that has no increase in its perfection necessarily exists. Because its existence in conception is less than its existence in reality, so it exists in reality. Because absolute perfection would be negated due to its non-existence, and nothing of perfection would remain, but rather absolute deficiency which is non-existence. So mere conception of perfection affirms its existence.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from perfection (burhan al-istikmal), also known as the argument from ideal forms (burhan al-muthul al-'ulya). The argument begins with an observation about the human mind: whenever it thinks of something great, it can always conceive of something greater. The mind cannot be satisfied with a limited degree of greatness without a cause, and it does not know why it should stop. Every perfect thing prompts the mind to seek a more perfect one, leading to an infinite regress until it reaches absolute perfection that admits no increase or deficiency. This absolutely perfect being must exist in reality, because if it were only in the mind, it would be less perfect than if it also existed externally. Moreover, absolute perfection cannot be nothing because non-existence is a deficiency. Thus, the very idea of perfection necessitates the existence of a perfectly real being.

Key Terms
برهان الاستكمال
برهان المثل العليا
كمال مطلق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that everything we conceive must exist in reality.
Correction: No, the argument specifically applies only to the concept of absolute perfection. For ordinary things, conceiving them does not entail their existence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from perfection, why must the absolutely perfect being exist in reality?
A) Because the mind cannot conceive of non-existence
B) Because existence in reality is more perfect than existence in conception alone
C) Because there must be a creator
D) Because the universe is perfect

Key Takeaway
The argument from perfection shows that the idea of absolute perfection cannot be a mere mental construct; it must refer to a real being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument reveals that nothing finite ever satisfies the human mind—it always seeks a greater perfection. What is one finite thing you have been treating as an ultimate end? How does this lesson call you to redirect your longing toward the Absolutely Perfect?
?
If the Absolutely Perfect must exist, then He is perfectly aware of you, perfectly powerful over you, and perfectly just with you. Does the reality of His presence frighten you or comfort you? What would you need to change in your life to live as if He is truly real and present right now?
?
Al-Aqqad says that non-existence is a deficiency, so absolute perfection cannot be non-existent. Have you ever acted as if God were absent or indifferent? In what area of your life are you functionally denying His existence? What step can you take today to acknowledge His reality in that area?

The Moral Proof: Conscience as Evidence
Al-Aqqad presents the moral argument based on conscience and duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moral proof uses the universal human sense of duty and conscience as evidence for the existence of God.

Core Idea
Our sense of moral obligation points to a divine lawgiver.

Al-Aqqad presents a rational argument based on an undeniable human experience: the feeling of moral duty, even when it goes against our desires. He asks a profound question: where does this sense of obligation come from, if not from God? This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its rhetorical style. Notice the key terms: الوازع الأخلاقي, الواجب, الضمير.
The passage uses rhetorical questions to make its point.
2
Understand the three signs
Identify the three terms that describe the same phenomenon: the moral deterrent, duty, and conscience. They all point to an inner sense of right and wrong.
3
Analyze the rhetorical questions
Al-Aqqad asks two questions: (1) Why do we hold ourselves accountable to truth? (2) Why do we feel obliged to do unpleasant duties even in secret? The implied answer is that God is the source of this obligation.
Consider the power of the second question: even when no one sees, we still feel duty.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that without a divine standard (ملكة الحق), these moral phenomena are inexplicable. Reflect on whether you find this argument convincing.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - وأما برهان الأخلاق : فهو علامة ، في النفس الإنسانية. لا يتأتي وجودها فيها وجود إله . وتلك هي علامة الوازع الأخلاقي، أو علامة الواجب أو علامة الضمير . فمن أين استوجب الإنسان أن يدين نفسه بالحق كما نعرفه . إن لم يكن في الكون قسطاس للحق ، يغرس في نفسه هذا الوجوب ؟ ومن أين تقرر في طبع الإنسان : أن الواجب الكريه لديه ، أولى به من إطاعة الهوى المحبب إليه ، وإن لم يطلع أحد على دخيلة سره ؟
4. As for the proof of morality: it is a sign in the human soul. Its occurrence in it does not occur [without] the existence of a god. And that is the sign of the moral deterrent, or the sign of duty, or the sign of conscience. So from where did man hold himself accountable to the truth as we know it, if there were not in the universe a balance of truth that plants this obligation in his soul? And from where was it determined in human nature that the unpleasant duty is more deserving of him than obeying beloved desire, even if no one found out his inner secret?
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage outlines the moral proof (burhan al-akhlaq). It begins by stating that morality is a sign (alama) within the human soul, and this sign cannot exist without the existence of a god. This sign is identified as the moral deterrent (al-wazi' al-akhlaqi), the sense of duty (al-wajib), or conscience (al-damir). The argument then poses two rhetorical questions: (1) How did man come to hold himself accountable to truth, if not for a divine standard of truth implanted in him? (2) How did it become ingrained in human nature that an unpleasant duty is more worthy of obedience than a beloved desire, even in secret? The implied answer is that only the existence of a moral lawgiver (God) can explain these universal moral phenomena.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moral proof is simply that people have a conscience.
Correction: The proof is not just that we have a conscience, but that the objective sense of moral obligation cannot be explained without a divine source.

Checkpoint
What phenomenon does the moral proof use as evidence for God's existence?
A) The order in nature
B) The human sense of duty and conscience
C) The existence of the universe

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In that moment, were you aware of a seeing Presence? How does that experience validate the moral proof from conscience? Now, ask yourself: if you consistently obey your conscience even in secret, are you not living out the very sign that points to your Creator? What would it mean for your faith if you ignored that inner call?
?
The passage asks: 'From where did man hold himself accountable to the truth?' Pause and consider: when you do something wrong in private, who is it that you feel accountable to? Is it merely your own standards, or something greater? If there were no ever-present Observer, would you still feel the weight of duty? Let your answer reveal where you truly stand with Allah. Are you living as if you are seen, or as if you are alone?

The Proof of Mutual Hindrance (Burhan al-Tamanu')
Al-Aqqad expounds the proof of mutual hindrance for God's unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof of mutual hindrance and why it demonstrates the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, they would hinder each other – proving only One can exist.

Al-Aqqad presents one of the most powerful rational arguments for God's unity: the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamanu'). Based on a Quranic verse, this argument uses a trilemma to show that two omnipotent beings cannot coexist. This lesson breaks down the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the trilemma structure.
2
Analyze the three possibilities
Identify the three scenarios regarding two gods' powers. Understand why each leads to a contradiction.
The three cases are: both sufficient, neither sufficient, only one sufficient.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the existence of two eternal gods is impossible and how the argument about identical attributes reinforces this.
This connects to the core idea of mutual hindrance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ب) ويذكر الآية القرآنية : ( لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا ) [الأنبياء ٢٢] ويقول : لن يقوم على ثبوت الوحدانية برهان أقوى من هذا البرهان. وهو برهان التمانع ، كما يسميه المتكلمون وأحسن من يشرحه هكذا . لا يخلو إما أن يكون قدرة كل واحد منهما وإرادته كافية في وجود العالم أو لا شيء منها كاف ، أو أحدهما كاف فقط. وعلى الأول يلزم اجتماع المؤثرين التامين على معلول واحد . وهو محال . وعلى الثاني يلزم عجزهما ، لأنها لا يمكن لهما التأثير إلا باشتراك الآخر . وعلى الثالث ألا يكون الآخر خالقا ، فلا يكون إلها . وصواب الأمر : إن وجود الإلهين سرمديين مستحيل . وإن بلوغ الكمال المطلق في صفة من الصفات يمنع بلوغ كمال مطلق آخر في تلك الصفة، وأن الاثنينية لا تتحقق في موجدين كلاهما لا بداية ولا نهاية ولا حدود ولا فروق . وكلاهما يريد ما يريده الآخر ، ويقدر ما يقدره ، ويعمل ما يعمله في كل حال ، وفي كل صغير وكبير . فهذان وجود واحد وليسا بوجودين . فإذا كانا اثنين لم يكونا إلا متمايزين متغايرين. فلا ينتظم على هذا التمايز والتغاير نظام واحد . وإذا كانا هما كاملين فالمخلوقات ناقصة ، ولا يكون تدبير المخلوق الناقص على وجه واحد ، بل على وجوه .
(B) And he mentions the Quranic verse: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' [Al-Anbiya 22]. And he says: 'No proof for the affirmation of unity is stronger than this proof. It is the proof of mutual hindrance, as the mutakallimun call it, and the best way to explain it is as follows: There is no escape from one of three possibilities: either the power and will of each of them is sufficient for the existence of the world, or neither of them is sufficient, or only one of them is sufficient. In the first case, there would be a combination of two complete causes upon a single effect, which is impossible. In the second case, both would be incapable, because they cannot affect except with the participation of the other. In the third case, the other would not be a creator, and thus would not be a god. The correct position is that the existence of two eternal gods is impossible. And that the attainment of absolute perfection in an attribute prevents the attainment of absolute perfection in that same attribute by another. And that duality cannot be realized in two existents both without beginning, without end, without limits, and without differences. And each of them wants what the other wants, and determines what the other determines, and does what the other does in every state and in every small and big thing. So these two are one existence and are not two existences. If they are two, they would only be distinct and different. Then on the basis of this distinction and difference, a single system cannot be ordered. And if they are both perfect, then the creatures are deficient, and the management of a deficient creature will not be in one manner, but in many manners.'
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamānu'), a classical argument used by the mutakallimun to establish the unity of God (tawhid). It begins by citing the Quranic verse that if there were multiple gods, the heavens and earth would be corrupted (Al-Anbiya 22). The argument proceeds by analyzing three logical possibilities regarding two supposed gods: (1) both are fully capable of creating the world independently — this would lead to two complete causes for a single effect, which is impossible; (2) neither is independently capable — then both are deficient and not gods; (3) only one is capable — then the other is not a creator and not a god. Thus, the only logical conclusion is that two eternal, absolutely perfect gods cannot exist together, because their attributes would clash. If both are absolutely perfect in every attribute, they would be identical in will and power, making them effectively one being. If they are distinct, their differences would prevent a unified order. Also, if both are perfect, the creation is imperfect, implying multiple plans, which is incoherent. Hence, only one God is possible.

Key Terms
برهان التمانع
توحيد
الوحدانية
مؤثرين تامين
إلهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance is about gods fighting or conflicting with each other.
Correction: The proof is a logical argument that any scenario with two omnipotent gods leads to a contradiction, not merely conflict.

Checkpoint
In the proof of mutual hindrance, what is the consequence if both supposed gods are fully capable of creating the world independently?
A) There would be two different worlds
B) They would cooperate
C) There would be two complete causes for one effect, which is impossible
D) They would fight

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance logically eliminates every possibility of two gods, leaving only the necessary existence of one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if you try to place any rival alongside Allah, chaos would result. Where in your life have you placed a 'rival' to Allah—something you fear or love as much as Him? How does this proof expose the internal corruption of that partnership?
?
If two gods would be either one (identical) or incapable, then the only real option is One God. Does your heart serve only One Master, or have you allowed multiple loyalties to compete? What would it take to make your soul's sole allegiance clear?
?
The proof states that two perfect beings would be identical in will and action. Are you trying to serve both Allah and your own desires? How does this passage expose the impossibility of such a divided heart?

The Proof of Mutual Exclusion (Burhan al-Tamānu')
The author presents and explains the proof of mutual exclusion for God's unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and logical force of the proof of mutual exclusion for God's unity.

Core Idea
Two omnipotent gods cannot coexist; the universe itself proves that only One is possible.

This lesson unpacks one of the most powerful rational arguments for tawheed—the proof of mutual exclusion (burhan al-tamānu'). The argument proceeds by examining what would happen if there were two gods, and shows that every possibility leads to an impossibility or contradiction. The proof is rooted in the Quranic verse (21:22) and is given a rigorous logical form by the theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read and observe the passage
Read the Arabic source and its English translation carefully. Notice how the author begins with a Quranic verse and then presents a logical analysis.
The proof is called 'burhan al-tamānu'' because it shows that two gods would mutually exclude each other.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The author lists three possibilities regarding the power and will of two supposed gods. Find these three cases in the text.
3
Analyze each scenario's outcome
For each of the three cases, understand why the author says it leads to impossibility or contradiction. Consider why the first case is impossible, the second implies incapability, and the third denies godhood.
This logical trilemma is the core of the proof.
4
Examine the deeper reasoning
After the trilemma, the author provides additional arguments about the impossibility of two eternal beings with absolute perfection. Notice how he links twoness with distinction and the inability to maintain a single order.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand how this proof establishes that only one God can exist, and that the observed order and perfection of the universe confirm this.
The proof is both scriptural and rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر الآية القرآنية : ( لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا ) [الأنبياء ٢٢] ويقول : لن يقوم على ثبوت الوحدانية برهان أقوى من هذا البرهان. وهو برهان التمانع ، كما يسميه المتكلمون وأحسن من يشرحه هكذا . لا يخلو إما أن يكون قدرة كل واحد منهما وإرادته كافية في وجود العالم أو لا شيء منها كاف ، أو أحدهما كاف فقط. وعلى الأول يلزم اجتماع المؤثرين التامين على معلول واحد . وهو محال . وعلى الثاني يلزم عجزهما، لأنها لا يمكن لهما التأثير إلا باشتراك الآخر . وعلى الثالث ألا يكون الآخر خالقا، فلا يكون إلها . وصواب الأمر : إن وجود المين سرمديين مستحيل . وإن بلوغ الكمال المطلق في صفة من الصفات يمنع بلوغ كمال مطلق آخر في تلك الصفة، وان الأثنينية و لا تتحقق في موجدين كلاهما يلا بداية ولا نهاية ولا حدود ولا فروق . وكلاهما يريد ما يريده الآخر، ويقدر ما يقدره، ويعمل ما يعمله في كل حال، وفي كل صغير وكبير . فهذان وجود واحد وليسا بوجودين . فإذا كانا اثنين لم يكونا إلا متمايزين متغايرين. فلا ينتظم على هذا التمايز والتغاير نظام واحد . وإذا كانا هما كاملين فالمخلوقات ناقصة، ولا يكون تدبير المخلوق الناقص على وجه واحد، بل على وجوه .
And he mentions the Quranic verse: (If there were in them [the heavens and the earth] gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined) [Al-Anbiya 22]. And he says: There will not stand a proof stronger than this proof for establishing unity. And it is the proof of mutual exclusion (burhan al-tamānu'), as the theologians call it, and the best way to explain it is as follows: It is inevitably either that each one's power and will are sufficient for the existence of the world, or none of them is sufficient, or only one of them is sufficient. On the first, it necessitates the concurrence of two full agents on one effect, which is impossible. On the second, it necessitates their incapability, because neither can effect except through the participation of the other. On the third, the other would not be a creator, and thus would not be a god. And the correct matter is: The existence of two eternal beings is impossible. And the attainment of absolute perfection in an attribute prevents another absolute perfection from attaining that same attribute. And twoness is not realized in two existents, both without beginning, without end, without limits, and without differences. And each wills what the other wills, and determines what the other determines, and does what the other does in every state, in every small and large matter. So these two are one existence, not two existences. If they are two, they are only distinct and different. Then upon this distinctness and difference, a single order cannot be established. And if they are both perfect, then created things are imperfect, and the management of the imperfect created thing is not upon one single way, but upon multiple ways.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the proof of mutual exclusion (burhan al-tamānu') for the unity of God. The author first cites the Quranic verse (21:22) which states that if there were multiple gods, the heavens and earth would be ruined. He then asserts that no proof stronger than this exists for establishing tawheed, and that the theologians call it 'burhan al-tamānu'. He explains it through a logical breakdown of possibilities regarding two supposed gods: either both are fully capable of creating the world, or neither is, or only one is. On the first scenario, two fully effective agents acting on one effect is impossible (this would entail concurrence of two perfect causes on one effect). On the second, both would be incapable, as each needs the other's participation. On the third, the other would not be a creator, hence not a god. The author then gives the correct view: the existence of two eternal beings is impossible because absolute perfection in an attribute prevents another being from possessing that same absolute perfection. Furthermore, twoness cannot be realized in two beings that are both eternal, limitless, and without differences; if they are truly two, they must be distinct and different, but then a single coherent order cannot be maintained. If both are perfect, then the imperfect creation cannot be managed in a single way, but only in multiple ways, which contradicts the notion of a single ordered universe.
