Al-Razi's Argument: Existence is One Concept
Al-Razi argues that existence is a single concept because conceptual distinction needs an external differentiator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that existence is a single concept, distinguished only by external attributes.

Core Idea
Existence is one; distinctions come from outside.

Al-Razi presents a clever argument: if necessary and possible existence were different in themselves, reason would distinguish them immediately, but it doesn't. This shows existence itself is a single shared concept, and any distinction comes from an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: analogy, conditional, conclusion.
The analogy with blackness and whiteness is key.
2
Understand the analogy
Al-Razi says we distinguish blackness and whiteness by their concepts alone. This is the model for how distinct concepts behave.
3
Follow the conditional argument
If existence of Necessary and existence of possible were distinct concepts, reason would distinguish them immediately. But it does not—only after adding external attribution. So they are not distinct in themselves.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فلو كان ... لكن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه ليس كذلك'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Existence as such is a single concept. Existents are only distinguished by something external to their essence.
This is the main takeaway: existence is univocal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : نحن إذا عقلنا معنى السواد ومعنى البياض، قضى العقل بامتياز معنى السواد عن معنى البياض بنفسه من غير أن يحتاج فيه إلى إلحاق قيد آخر، بهما يوجب الامتياز. فلو كان وجود الواجب، حقيقة مخالفة لوجود الممكن، لكنا إذا تصورنا هذين الوجودين، وجب أن تحكم بديهة العقل بامتياز أحدهما عن الآخر، لكن من المعلوم بالضرورة أنه ليس كذلك، فإنا ما لم نصف أحد الموجودين إلى الواجب، والموجود الآخر إلى الممكن، لم يحصل في العقل امتياز أحد الموجودين عن الآخر. فعلمنا أن الموجودات في أنفسها ليست متمايزة من حيث إنها وجودات، بل الوجود من حيث إنه وجود، مفهوم واحد، وأنه إنما يمتاز بعضها عن بعض بسبب خارج عن ماهيتها.
The seventh: When we conceive the meaning of blackness and the meaning of whiteness, reason judges the distinction of the meaning of blackness from the meaning of whiteness by itself without needing to attach another predicate that would cause distinction. So if the existence of the Necessary were a reality different from the existence of the possible, then when we conceive these two existences, the innate reason would necessarily judge the distinction of one from the other. But it is known necessarily that it is not so, for unless we attribute one of the two existents to the Necessary and the other existent to the possible, no distinction of one existent from the other occurs in the mind. Thus we know that existents in themselves are not distinct insofar as they are existences, rather existence insofar as it is existence is a single concept, and that they are only distinguished from one another by something external to their essence.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof that existence is a single concept. Al-Razi (the author) argues by analogy: when we conceive blackness and whiteness, our innate reason distinguishes them immediately without needing an external qualifier. If existence of the Necessary Being (wajib) and existence of the possible (mumkin) were different realities, then upon conceiving both, reason would naturally distinguish them. But experience shows this is not the case: we only distinguish the two existents when we additionally attribute one to the Necessary and the other to the possible. Therefore, the distinction between existents does not come from existence itself, but from something external to their essence. Hence existence is a single shared concept.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
واجب (wajib)
ممكن (mumkin)
بديهة العقل (badihah al-'aql)
امتياز (imtiyaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the distinction between necessary and possible existence implies different meanings of 'existence'.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the distinction is due to external attributes, not existence itself, so 'existence' is a single concept.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh argument, why do we not immediately distinguish between the existence of the Necessary and the existence of the possible?
A) Because they are identical in essence
B) Because our innate reason does not operate on such abstract concepts
C) Because we need an external attribution to distinguish them
D) Because existence is not a real concept

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single concept; the distinction between necessary and possible existence is due to external attributes, not existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is a single concept, and the only thing that separates your existence from that of the Creator is an external attribute (your dependence on Him), then in what moments today did you live as though your existence were self-sufficient? How will you remind yourself tomorrow that you exist only because He sustains you?
?
Al-Razi says we cannot distinguish between necessary and possible existence without mentally attributing one to the Necessary and the other to the possible. In your daily life, do you attribute your existence to Allah or do you implicitly attribute it to yourself? What habit can you adopt to make this attribution conscious and constant?
?
The passage argues that existence itself does not admit degrees or differences—distinctions come from outside. How does this affect your understanding of God's oneness? If you truly believed that all existence shares the same conceptual reality, how would your worship of Allah change?

The Eighth Proof: Consistency of the Need for a Creator
Al-Razi argues that the concept of existence remains unchanged regardless of the nature of the creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi argues that the constancy of the need for a creator, regardless of the creator's nature, demonstrates that existence is a single concept.

Core Idea
The need for a creator is uniform, so existence is one.

Al-Razi presents a unique argument: after establishing that the world needs a creator, he examines different possible natures of that creator. Surprisingly, the fundamental belief that the world requires a creator remains unchanged across all scenarios. This constancy reveals something profound about the concept of existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the logical flow from the established proof that the world is originated to the various assumptions about the creator.
2
Identify the assumptions
List the different possibilities Al-Razi considers about the creator: eternal or originated, necessary by itself or by cause, body or accident or neither.
These are the 'all divisions' he refers to.
3
Observe the constant
Despite the different assumptions, the belief that the world needs a creator remains constant and unchanged. Ask yourself why this constancy is significant.
4
Connect to existence
Understand the conclusion: this constancy indicates that existence itself is a single meaning shared by all the different possibilities. The concept of being an existent in external entities is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : إنا إذا أقمنا الدليل على أن العالم محدث، قضى العقل بافتقاره إلى موجود يوجده، ثم بعد ذلك يقع البحث في أن ذلك الموجود الذي أوجد العالم، هل هو قديم أو محدث؟ وبتقدير أن يكون قديما، فهل هو واجب لذاته، أو واجب بسبب وجوب علته؟ وهل هو جسم أو حال في الجسم؟ أو لا جسم ولا حال في الجسم؟ وعلى جميع التقديرات فإن اعتقاد أن العالم لا بد له من موجد يوجده، ومن كائن يكونه، باقي ثابت غير متغير، وكل ذلك يدل على أن المعقول من كونه موجودا متحققا في الأعيان: أمر واحد مشترك فيه بين كل الأقسام.
The eighth: When we establish the proof that the world is originated, reason judges its need for an existent that brings it into existence. Then after that, inquiry occurs concerning that existent that brought the world into existence: is it eternal or originated? And if it is assumed to be eternal, is it necessary by itself, or necessary due to the necessity of its cause? And is it a body or an accident in a body, or neither a body nor an accident? In all these assumptions, the belief that the world must have a creator that creates it and a maker that makes it remains constant and unchanged. And all that indicates that the intelligible from its being an existent realized in external entities is a single matter shared among all divisions.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof. Al-Razi begins with the established proof that the world is originated (muhdath), which entails its need for a creator (mujid). Then various possibilities about that creator are considered: is it eternal or originated? If eternal, is it necessary by itself or by another? Is it a body, an accident, or neither? Regardless of which assumption is made, the core belief that the world requires a creator remains constant and unchanged. This constancy of the need for a creator, whatever its nature, shows that the concept of 'being an existent realized in external entities' is a single meaning common to all those divisions. Thus, existence is one concept.

Key Terms
عالَم
محدَث
موجِد
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different possible natures of the creator imply different concepts of existence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the need for a creator is uniform across all possibilities, indicating that existence itself is a single concept shared by all.

Checkpoint
What remains constant according to the eighth argument, regardless of the creator's nature?
A) The creator's necessity by itself
B) The world's need for a creator
C) The creator's being a body
D) The creator's being eternal

Key Takeaway
The need for a creator is consistent across all assumptions, proving that existence is a single concept.

?

?
You must ask yourself: Is your belief in God dependent on your intellectual assumptions, or is it as unshakable as the proof that the world needs a creator? What step will you take to anchor your faith in that constant need?

The Ninth Proof: Existence is a Single Concept
Al-Razi argues that existence is a single concept by showing it is compatible with contradictory attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that existence is a single concept because it does not contradict any specific attribute.

Core Idea
Existence remains the same across contradictory attributes.

In this proof, Al-Razi uses a simple observation about our concepts: the idea of 'existence' does not clash with any particular attribute, even when those attributes contradict each other. This shows that existence is a distinct, single concept underlying all things. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage slowly once. Notice how Al-Razi sets up two pairs of contradictory concepts before drawing his conclusion.
The pairs are: necessary of existence vs possible of existence, and body occupying space vs immaterial existent.
2
Identify the contradictory pairs
List the two pairs of attributes that Al-Razi calls contradictory. Understand why each pair cannot be true of the same thing at the same time.
Necessary and possible are exclusive; material and immaterial are exclusive.
3
Observe the neutrality of existence
Notice that Al-Razi says 'being an existent realized in external entities' does not contradict any of these pairs. It can be combined with each one.
This neutrality is the key to the argument: existence is a separate concept that is always compatible.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that existence is a single concept (mafhum wahid) that does not vary with different forms. Appreciate why this matters for understanding the nature of existence.
This proof argues against those who think existence is multiple or ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : إن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن المعقول من كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ينافي المعقول من كونه ممكن الوجود لذاته وبالعكس. وأيضا المعقول من كونه جسما متحيزا، ينافي المعقول من كونه موجودا مجردا، فجميع هذه الاعتبارات متناقضة متغايرة، وصريح العقل شاهد بأن المعقول من كونه موجودا محصلا في الأعيان، لا يناقض شيئا من هذه الاعتبارات ولا ينافيها. وذلك يدل على أن كونه موجودا، مفهوم مغاير لهذه الاعتبارات المخصوصة باقي مع كل واحد منها. وذلك يدل على أن المفهوم من كونه موجودا مفهوما واحدا لا يختلف باختلاف هذه الصور.
The ninth: Innate reason witnesses that the intelligible of being necessary of existence by itself is contrary to the intelligible of being possible of existence by itself and vice versa. Also, the intelligible of being a body occupying space is contrary to the intelligible of being an immaterial existent. So all these considerations are contradictory and different, and plain reason witnesses that the intelligible of being an existent realized in external entities does not contradict any of these considerations nor is it contrary to them. That indicates that 'being existent' is a concept different from these specific considerations and remains with each one of them. And that indicates that the concept of 'being existent' is a single concept that does not differ with the difference of these forms.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof. Al-Razi notes that innate reason tells us that the concept of 'necessary of existence by itself' contradicts 'possible of existence by itself'. Similarly, being a body occupying space contradicts being immaterial. These specific considerations are contradictory. However, the concept of 'being an existent realized in external entities' does not contradict any of these contradictory considerations; it remains compatible with all. Therefore, the meaning of existence is distinct from these specific attributes and remains constant across them. Hence, existence is a single concept that does not vary with varying forms.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (necessary of existence)
ممكن الوجود (possible of existence)
جسم متحيز (body occupying space)
مجرد (immaterial)
مفهوم (concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think existence is different for each kind of thing (e.g., God's existence is different from a human's).
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the concept of existence is the same across all attributes; it does not change even when the attributes are contradictory.

Checkpoint

B) It is identical to one of them
C) It does not contradict any of them
D) It cancels them out

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single, neutral concept that does not contradict any specific attribute, proving its unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Razi shows that existence remains constant across contradictory attributes. What would it look like to live today with the conviction that your existence is a neutral gift from the Necessary Existent?
?
The proof argues that existence is not opposed to anything. Yet your heart often opposes the trials that come with your existence. How can you surrender to the fact that your existence itself is a sign of Allah's will, independent of the circumstances? Will you commit to thanking Him not just for what you have, but for the very fact that you are?

The Tenth Proof: Existence as a Shared Meaning
Al-Razi provides the tenth proof and concludes existence is a shared meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi proves that existence is a single meaning shared between the Necessary and the possible, and why denying it leads to self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Claiming existence is not shared self-destructs; it must be a single meaning.

Al-Razi presents his tenth and final proof to establish that existence is not merely a homonymous term but a single meaning shared by all existents. He demonstrates that denying this leads to a logical contradiction. This conclusion is crucial for understanding the nature of divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow.
The argument uses conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the self-defeating argument
Find where Al-Razi shows that the statement 'existence is not shared' would itself be false if existence were not a single concept. Understand the logical contradiction.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Recognize that the term 'existence' applied to God and creatures conveys a shared meaning, not mere homonymy. This is the key outcome.
This connects to the broader discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إنا إذا قلنا : الوجود غير مشترك فيه بين جميع الموجودات. فهذا الحكم إنما يتناول جميع الموجودات، لو كان المفهوم من الوجود من حيث إنه وجود، مفهوما واحدا. إذ لو كان الوجود له مفهومات كثيرة، فلعل هذا المفهوم، وإن كان غير مشترك فيه بين الموجودات، فالمفهوم الآخر يكون مشتركا فيه بين كل الموجودات. فيثبت أن قولنا : الوجود غير مشترك فيه، إنما يعم هذا الحكم، وثبت في كل موجود، ولو ثبت أن الوجود مشترك فيه يثبت : أن قولنا : الوجود غير مشترك فيه : كلام، يفضي ثبوته إلى نفيه. فيكون باطلا. واعلم أنا قد استقصينا هذه المسألة في أول كتاب الوجود وأحكامه. إلا زوائد كثيرة في هذا الموضع، حتى يكون الكلام في هذه المسألة مجموعا في موضع واحد. وثبت بهذه البراهين : أن لفظ الوجود الواقع على الواجب لذاته وعلى الممكن لذاته ليس بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط، بل بحسب أنه يفيد معنى واحدا مشتركا فيه بين الضدين.
The tenth: If we say: 'Existence is not shared among all existents,' this judgment only applies to all existents if the concept of existence insofar as it is existence is a single concept. For if existence had many concepts, perhaps this concept, even if it is not shared among existents, the other concept would be shared among all existents. Thus it is established that our statement 'existence is not shared' only generalizes this judgment, and it is established in every existent. And if it were established that existence is shared, it would be established that our statement 'existence is not shared' is a statement whose affirmation leads to its negation, so it is false. And know that we have exhaustively discussed this issue in the beginning of the Book of Existence and Its Rulings, except that we added much here so that the discussion on this issue is collected in one place. And it is established by these proofs that the term 'existence' applied to the Necessary by itself and to the possible by itself is not merely according to homonymy, but rather insofar as it conveys a single meaning shared between the two opposites.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage contains the tenth proof and the conclusion. Al-Razi argues that if someone says 'existence is not shared among all existents,' this statement only covers all existents if existence is actually a single concept. If existence had multiple meanings, it could be that some meanings are not shared while others are. So the claim 'existence is not shared' would be self-defeating because if existence is not shared, then the statement is false for some meaning; but if existence is shared, then the statement is false. Hence the claim is false. Thus we conclude that existence is a single concept common to all. The author notes that he has covered this topic elsewhere but repeats it here for completeness. The final conclusion: the term 'existence' when applied to the Necessary and the possible is not mere homonymy; rather, it conveys a single meaning shared between these two opposites (the Necessary and the possible).

Key Terms
اشتراك لفظي (homonymy)
اشتراك معنوي (shared meaning)
ضدان (opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim existence is purely homonymous (اشتراك لفظي) between God and creatures.
Correction: Al-Razi proves it is a single shared meaning (اشتراك معنوي) because the homonymous view leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What does the conclusion state about the term 'existence' as applied to the Necessary and the possible?
A) It is purely homonymous
B) It is a single shared meaning
C) It is ambiguous
D) It is not applicable to the possible

Key Takeaway
The term 'existence' is not merely homonymous; it conveys a single meaning shared between the Necessary and the possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists' and 'a tree exists,' do you really believe the word 'exists' means the same thing? If yes, how does that change your awareness of God's presence in the world? If no, what does that imply about the distance between you and Him?
?
Al-Razi proves that existence is a shared meaning between the Necessary and the possible. If your very existence shares the same reality as God's in some way, how should that affect your daily sense of accountability to Him?
?
You have just learned that the claim 'existence is not shared' is self-defeating. Are there areas in your life where you treat your existence as independent of God, as if it were not shared with His sustaining power? What would it cost you to live today as if your existence is a borrowed gift from the One who truly exists?

Two Opinions on God's Existence and Refutation of the First
The author presents two opinions on God's existence and refutes the first with a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing views on the nature of God's existence and how a logical dilemma refutes the opinion that God's existence is identical to His essence without a quiddity.

Core Idea
A logical dilemma shows that existence cannot be self-subsistent by itself.

When discussing God's existence, theologians debated whether His existence is identical to His essence or an attribute added to it. In this passage, the author presents both positions and refutes the first with a simple but powerful logical dilemma. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage contains two opinions and a proof.
2
Identify the two opinions
Name the two opinions presented: one that God's reality is existence itself, another that existence is an accident to His quiddity.
The first opinion includes a condition: existence not being an accident of any quiddity.
3
Analyze the logical dilemma
The proof asks whether existence qua existence requires self-subsistence or not. Consider both possibilities and their consequences.
Each horn leads to an unacceptable outcome for the first opinion.
4
Understand the refutation
The first horn would make all existents self-subsistent (contradicting contingency), the second would make no existent self-subsistent (denying God's independence). Thus the first opinion is false.
The author claims the argument is so clear that rational instinct accepts it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: بقي لنا في المسألة قولان:
أحدهما: قول من يقول: تمام حقيقة الله تعالى هو هذا الوجود، بشرط كونه غير عارض بشيء من الماهيات.
والثاني: قول من يقول: إن وجود الله تعالى صفة عارضة لماهيته.
فنقول: القول الأول باطل، والذي يدل عليه وجوه:
الحجة الأولى: أن نقول: الوجود من حيث إنه وجود إما أن يقتضي أن يكون قائما بنفسه، مستقلا بذاته، غنيا عن كل ما سواه. أو لا يقتضي ذلك. فإن كان الأول وجب أن يكون كل وجود كذلك، فجميع الموجودات تكون قائمة بأنفسها، مستقلة بحقائقها، واجبة التقرير بما هي هي، وهذا ظاهر البطلان، وإلا لزم أن لا يكون شيء من الممكنات موجودا أصلا. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا يكون قائما بنفسه ولا مستقلا بحقيقته، فوجب أن لا يكون في الوجود موجود قائم بنفسه مستقل بحقيقته، وذلك مناقض لقول من يقول: إن وجود الله تعالى قائم بنفسه، مستقل بحقيقته من حيث إنه هو. وهذا الكلام بلغ في الجلاء والوضوح إلى حيث تشهد فطرة العقلاء وغريزتهم بأنه لا يقبل السؤال والاعتراض البتة.
So we say: There remain for us in the matter two opinions: One of them: the opinion of one who says: The entire reality of God is this existence, on condition that it is not an accident of any quiddity. And the second: the opinion of one who says: The existence of God is an attribute accident to His quiddity. So we say: The first opinion is false, and what indicates it are proofs: The first proof: that we say: Existence qua existence either requires that it be self-subsistent, independent in essence, free from need of anything else; or it does not require that. If the first, then every existence must be like that, so all existents would be self-subsistent, independent in their realities, necessarily determined as what they are, and this is clearly false, otherwise it would necessitate that no contingent thing would exist at all. And if the second, then existence qua existence is not self-subsistent nor independent in its reality, so it would be necessary that no existent in existence be self-subsistent nor independent in its reality, and that contradicts the statement of one who says that the existence of God is self-subsistent and independent in its reality insofar as it is He. And this discourse has reached such clarity and obviousness that the innate disposition and instinct of rational people witness that it absolutely does not admit question or objection.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents two competing views on the nature of God's existence and offers a refutation of the first view. The two opinions are: (1) that God's entire reality is simply this existence itself, provided that existence is not an accident supervening on any quiddity; (2) that God's existence is an attribute that is accidental to His quiddity. The author declares the first opinion false and provides a proof. The proof is a dilemma: Existence considered purely as existence either requires that it be self-subsistent and independent (qaim bi-nafsih, mustaqill bi-dhatih) or it does not. If it does, then every existent would be self-subsistent and independent, which is absurd because contingent things are not; indeed, no contingent thing would exist. If it does not, then no existent would be self-subsistent and independent, which contradicts the claim that God's existence is self-subsistent and independent. Thus the first opinion leads to one of two unacceptable consequences. The author asserts that this argument is so clear that any rational person's innate disposition (fitra) and instinct recognize it as irrefutable.

Key Terms
تمام حقيقة الله
الوجود
عارض
الماهية
قائم بنفسه
مستقل بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first opinion is refuted simply by asserting the second opinion's premise.
Correction: The refutation is a logical dilemma independent of the second opinion, showing that existence cannot be self-subsistent in itself.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof's key premise about existence qua existence?
A) It is always accidental to a quiddity.
B) It either requires self-subsistence or it does not.
C) It is always self-subsistent.
D) It is never self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
The first opinion on God's existence is refuted by a logical dilemma that forces an unacceptable consequence no matter which alternative is chosen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence itself is not inherently self-subsistent, then every self-subsistent thing — including God — must have that self-subsistence from His very essence, not from the mere fact of existing. How does this deepen your awe of God's absolute uniqueness? Are there areas of your life where you treat something other than God as self-sufficient, expecting it to sustain you without needing Him?
?
The author claims the argument is so clear that rational instinct recognizes it. Search your own heart: does your mind fully submit to this logical necessity, or do you find yourself resistant? If there is resistance, could it be because you want to hold onto a concept of God that fits your own reasoning rather than the truth revealed through this proof? What change in your thinking is this argument calling you to make today?

Two Opinions on God's Existence and the First Proof
The author states the two opinions on God's existence and gives the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on the nature of God's existence and the first proof that refutes the view that existence itself constitutes God's essence.

Core Idea
Existence as such cannot be both self-subsistent and accidental — a dilemma leads to absurdity.

This passage presents two opposing views on whether God's existence is His essence or an attribute. The author then introduces a powerful proof that dismantles the first view using a logical dilemma. By working through this argument, you'll see how kalam reasoning builds on fundamental distinctions about existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the two opinions
Notice how the first view says God's reality is existence itself without being accidental, while the second says existence is an attribute of His essence.
2
Understand the author's initial move
The author declares the first opinion false and introduces proofs. Focus on the first proof's dilemma.
3
Follow the dilemma
Existence as such either entails self-subsistence or not. Trace both branches to their conclusions.
4
See the contradiction
If existence entails self-subsistence, all existents would be self-subsistent, which is false. If not, then no existent is self-subsistent, contradicting God's nature.
5
Reflect on the author's claim of clarity
The author insists this argument is intuitively clear. Ask yourself why he says that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : بقي لنا في المسألة قولان : أحدهما : قول من يقول : تمام حقيقة الله تعالى هو هذا الوجود ، بشرط كونه غير عارض بشيء من الماهيات والثاني : قول من يقول إن وجود الله تعالى صفة عارضة لماهيته . فنقول : القول الأول باطل ، والذي يدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : الوجود من حيث إنه وجود إما أن يقتضي أن يكون قائما بنفسه ، مستقلا بذاته ، غنيا عن كل ما سواه . أو لا يقتضي ذلك فإن كان الأول وجب أن يكون كل وجود كذلك ، فجميع الموجودات تكون قائمة بأنفسها ، مستقلة بحقائقها ، واجبة التفرد بما هي هي ، وهذا ظاهر البطلان ، وإلا لزم أن لا يكون شيء من الممكنات موجودا أصلا ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ الوجود من حيث إنه وجود لا يكون قائما بنفسه ولا مستقلا بحقيقته ، فوجب أن لا يكون في الوجود موجود قائم بنفسه مستقل بحقيقته وذلك مناقض لقول من يقول : إن وجود الله تعالى قائم بنفسه ، مستقل بحقيقته من حيث إنه هو . وهذا الكلام بلغ في الجلاء والوضوح إلى حيث تشهد فطرة العقلاء وغريزتهم بأنه لا يقبل السؤال والاعتراض البتة .
We say: There remain for us two opinions on the matter: The first: the opinion of those who say: The complete reality of Allah Most High is this existence, with the condition of it not being an accident of any of the essences. And the second: the opinion of those who say: Indeed the existence of Allah Most High is an attribute accidental to His essence. So we say: The first opinion is false, and what indicates it are aspects: The first proof: That we say: Existence insofar as it is existence either entails that it is self-subsistent, independent in its essence, self-sufficient from everything other than it, or it does not entail that. If the first, then it is necessary that every existence be like that, so all existents would be self-subsistent, independent in their realities, necessarily unique in what they are, and this is clearly false, otherwise it would necessitate that none of the contingent things exist at all. And if the second, then existence insofar as it is existence is not self-subsistent nor independent in its reality, so it would be necessary that there be in existence no existent that is self-subsistent, independent in its reality. And this contradicts the statement of one who says: Indeed the existence of Allah is self-subsistent, independent in its reality insofar as it is He. And this speech has reached such clarity and obviousness that the innate disposition and instinct of rational people testifies that it absolutely does not accept any question or objection.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage begins by stating there are two opinions remaining for discussion. The first opinion holds that the complete reality of God is this existence itself, with the condition that it is not an accident of any essence. The second opinion holds that God's existence is an accidental attribute of His essence. The author declares the first opinion false and offers several proofs, beginning with the first proof. The first proof is a dilemma: existence as such either entails self-subsistence, independence, and sufficiency, or it does not. If it does, then every existent would be self-subsistent, which is false because contingent beings are not self-subsistent. If it does not, then no existent would be self-subsistent, contradicting the claim that God's existence is self-subsistent. The author claims this argument is so clear that it is intuitively accepted by rational people.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof only applies to physical objects or material existence.
Correction: The argument is about existence as such, not limited to physical things; it applies to all modes of existence including conceptual.

Checkpoint

A) Only God is self-subsistent.
B) All existents would be self-subsistent.
C) No existent would be self-subsistent.
D) Existence would be an accident of essences.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that existence cannot be inherently self-subsistent without making all existents self-subsistent, nor can it be accidental without making no existent self-subsistent, thereby forcing a distinction between God and contingent beings.

?
The proof shows that existence cannot be accidental to God's essence—He is existence itself. Do you ever think of God's attributes as separate from His essence, as if He could be without them? How can you practically worship a God whose very reality is existence? Commit to one specific act of worship today that reminds you of His self-sufficiency.

The Second Proof: A Trilemma on Existence and Accidence
A trilemma showing existence cannot be inherently accidental or non-accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against the view that existence is an accident, which presents a trilemma showing that existence cannot be inherently accidental or non-accidental.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be inherently accidental or non-accidental; each option leads to absurdity.

This passage presents the second proof, which is similar to the first but uses a more systematic trilemma. The argument forces us to consider three possibilities regarding existence: that it is inherently an accident, that it is inherently non-accidental, or that it is neutral. By showing each possibility leads to an unacceptable conclusion for the Necessary Existent, the proof demonstrates that existence cannot be an accident of essences. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall structure of the argument, which considers three options for existence.
Notice that the author sets up a clear three-way division (إما... أو... أو).
2
Identify the first horn
The first option is that existence entails being an accident of an essence. Consider what this implies for God's existence.
If true, even God's existence would be accidental to His essence, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.
3
Analyze the second horn
The second option is that existence entails not being an accident. Examine why this leads to absurdity regarding contingent things and the term 'existence'.
This horn forces either non-existence of contingent things or verbal homonymy, both absurd.
4
Examine the third horn
The third option is that existence is neutral. Understand why this would make God dependent on a separate cause.
If neutral, then any determination of existence requires an external cause, making the Necessary Existent dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهي قريبة مما تقدم. أن نقول : الوجود من حيث إنه وجود إما أن يقتضي أن يكون عارضا لماهية من الماهيات ، أو يقتضي أن يكون غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات، أو لا يقتضي ، لا العروض ولا التجرد . فإن كان الحق هو الأول ، فكل وجود فهو عارض لماهية من الماهيات ، ووجود واجب الوجود كذلك . وإن كان الحق هو الثاني ، فكل وجود فهو مجرد غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات ، فيلزم أن يقال : هذه الممكنات ليست موجودة أصلا، وإن كانت موجودة كانت موجودة بوجودات هي أعيانها. فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون لفظ الوجود واقعا على الوجودات بالاشتراك اللفظي ، وهو محال . وإن كان الحق هو الثالث ، فحينئذ يمتنع أن يصير الوجود متقيدا بقيد كونه عارضا تارة، وبقيد كونه لا عارضا أخرى ، إلا بسبب منفصل ، فيكون وجود واجب الوجود لذاته متعلقا بسبب منفصل ، فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود بغيره . وهو محال
The second proof: And it is close to what preceded. We say: Existence insofar as it is existence either entails that it is an accident of an essence among essences, or entails that it is not an accident of any essence, or does not entail either being an accident or being free. If the correct is the first, then every existence is an accident of an essence, and the existence of the Necessary Existent is likewise. If the correct is the second, then every existence is free, not an accident of any essence, so it follows that one says: These contingent things do not exist at all, and if they exist they exist by existences that are their very selves. Then it follows that the word 'existence' applies to existences by verbal homonymy, which is absurd. And if the correct is the third, then it is impossible for existence to become restricted by the restriction of being an accident sometimes and by the restriction of not being an accident at other times, except by a separate cause. Then the existence of the Necessary Existent by itself would be dependent on a separate cause, so the Necessary Existent by itself would be necessary by another, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is similar to the first. It sets up a trilemma: existence as such either entails being an accident of an essence, or entails not being an accident of any essence, or does not entail either. If the first, then God's existence would also be an accident, contradicting His necessity. If the second, then all existents are non-accidental, meaning contingent things either do not exist (since they are not self-subsistent) or their existence is identical to their essence, leading to verbal homonymy (the term 'existence' would mean something different for God and creatures), which is absurd. If the third, then existence has no inherent determination regarding being an accident or not, so any such determination must come from an external cause. This would make God's existence dependent on an external cause, meaning He would be necessary by another, which is impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One of the three options might be true and acceptable, such as the third option (neutrality).
Correction: The proof shows each option leads to an absurdity regarding the Necessary Existent, so none can be true. The only way out is to reject the premise that existence is an accident of essences.

C) God's existence would be non-accidental.
D) God's existence would be independent of essences.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses a trilemma to show that existence cannot be inherently accidental or non-accidental, thereby refuting the view that existence is an accident of essences.

The third option fails because it would make God dependent on a separate cause. In what areas of your life do you treat God as if He is not self-sufficient, expecting Him to act according to your timeline or desires? What would it mean to truly submit to the Necessary Existent who depends on nothing?

The Third Proof: Three Possible Determinants
The proof states its assumption and three possible determinants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the third proof and the three possibilities for what determines God's existence being non-accidental.

Core Idea
The third proof eliminates all possible causes for God's freedom from essence.

This lesson introduces the third proof against the view that God's existence is an accident of essence. The proof begins by assuming that God's existence is free from being an accident of any essence. It then asks what determines this freedom and self-sufficiency. Three possibilities are considered: the nature of existence itself, a state inherent in that existence, or something neither state nor locus. All are argued to be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the proof
Read the first part of the passage where the assumption and the three possibilities are stated. Notice how the proof sets up a trilemma.
2
Identify the three possibilities
List the three possibilities: (1) the nature of existence as such, (2) a state in that existence, (3) something neither state nor locus.
These cover all logical possibilities.
3
Understand the assumption
The proof assumes that God's existence is not an accident of any essence. This is a given for this argument.
4
Prepare for the refutations
The next lesson will examine how each possibility is refuted. For now, grasp the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : وهي أيضا قريبة مما تقدم : إذا قلنا : إن وجود واجب الوجود مجردا عن كونه عارضا بشيء من الماهيات ، فالمقتضى لهذا التجرد ، ولهذا الاستغناء إما طبيعة الوجود من حيث إنه وجود وإما أن يكون حالا فيه ، وإما أن لا يكون حالا فيه ولا محلا له ، والكل باطل ، أما بيان بطلان القسم الأول فلأنه يلزم أن يكون كل وجود كذلك ، لأن لازم الماهية مشترك فيه بين جميع أفراد تلك الماهية ، وأما بيان بطلان القسم الثاني : فلأن تلك الصفة الحالة في ذلك الوجود ، إما أن تكون لازمة لذلك الوجود ، أو لا تكون لازمة له ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يعود إلى التقسيم في أن المقتضى لذلك اللزوم إما نفس طبيعة الوجود، وإما أن يكون حالا فيها أو عملا الهام أو ما لا يكون حالا فيها ولا محلا لها . فإن كان ذلك بسبب حال آخر . لزم التسلسل وهو محال ، وأما إن كانت تلك الصفة الحالة في ذلك الوجود ليست لازمة لذلك الوجود، فحينئذ يكون ممكن الزوال ، وبتقدير زواله فإنه يجب زوال ذلك الاستغناء ، ويجب زوال ذلك الاستقلال ، فحينئذ يصير الواجب لذاته ، ممكنا لذاته وهو محال . وأما بيان بطلان القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون المقتضى لذلك التجرد ، ولذلك الاستغناء شيء يكون محلا لذلك الوجود أو حالا فيه؟ إن كان محلا للوجود كالحال فيه، فهو يرجع إلى القسم الثاني، وإن كان لا محلا ولا حالا، فهو غير معقول .
The third proof: And it is also close to what preceded: If we say: Indeed the existence of the Necessary Existent is free from being an accident of any of the essences, then the determinant of this being free and of this self-sufficiency is either the nature of existence insofar as it is existence, or it is a state in it, or it is neither a state in it nor a locus for it, and all are false. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the first division: because it necessitates that every existence be like that, for the necessary concomitant of an essence is common among all individuals of that essence. And as for the demonstration of the falsity of the second division: because that attribute which is a state in that existence, either is necessary for that existence or is not necessary for it. The first is false, because it returns to the division: the determinant of that necessity is either the nature of existence itself, or it is a state in it or an act of [؟] or what is neither a state in it nor a locus for it. If that is due to another state, an infinite regress follows, which is absurd. And if that attribute which is a state in that existence is not necessary for that existence, then it is possible of removal, and if it were removed, then that self-sufficiency must be removed, and that independence must be removed, so then the Necessary Existent by itself would become contingent by itself, which is absurd. And as for the demonstration of the falsity of the third division: that the determinant of that being free and that self-sufficiency is something that is a locus for that existence or a state in it? If it is a locus for existence like a state in it, then it reverts to the second division. And if it is neither a locus nor a state, then it is unintelligible.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage provides the third proof. It assumes that God's existence is free from being an accident of any essence. It then asks what determines this freedom and self-sufficiency. Three possibilities are considered: (1) The nature of existence as such. (2) A state (attribute) inherent in that existence. (3) Something that is neither a state nor a locus for it. The first is false because if existence per se entailed freedom, then every existence would be free, which is not true for contingent beings. The second is analyzed: if the state is necessary for that existence, then the necessity of that state leads to the same division (to avoid infinite regress), but if the state is not necessary, then it is possible to remove it, which would remove the self-sufficiency, making the Necessary Existent contingent, an absurdity. The third is either reducible to the second or unintelligible. Thus all options fail, supporting the refutation of the first opinion.

The Third Proof: Refuting the Three Possibilities
The proof refutes each of the three possibilities in turn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the three possibilities is refuted, leading to the conclusion that the first opinion is false.

Core Idea
All three possible determinants lead to absurdity, so God's freedom from essence is uncaused.

This lesson examines the refutations of the three possibilities presented in the third proof. The first possibility (nature of existence) would make all existence free, which is false. The second possibility (a state) either leads to infinite regress or makes God contingent. The third possibility (neither state nor locus) is either reducible to the second or unintelligible. Thus all fail.

Guided Path
1
Review the three possibilities
Recall from the previous lesson the three possible determinants. This lesson shows why each is false.
2
Follow the refutation of the first
The first possibility (nature of existence) is refuted because if existence per se entailed freedom, then every existence would be free, but contingent existence is not free.
This uses the principle that a necessary concomitant of an essence is shared by all individuals of that essence.
3
Follow the refutation of the second
The second possibility (a state) is analysed: if the state is necessary, it leads to the same division and potential infinite regress; if it is not necessary, its removal would remove self-sufficiency, making God contingent.
This is a crucial argument.
4
Follow the refutation of the third
The third possibility (neither state nor locus) is either reducible to the second or unintelligible, so it is also false.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since all possibilities are false, the assumption that God's existence is an accident of essence is refuted.
This completes the third proof.

The Third Proof: Refuting Existence as Accident
Presenting the third proof against the notion that God's existence is an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that refutes the claim that God's existence is an accident by showing that any possible explanation for its independence leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
The third proof exposes the impossibility of God's existence being an accident through a fourfold division and refutation.

This lesson presents the third proof in a series refuting the idea that God's existence is an accident added to His essence. The author uses a sophisticated argument by division: he considers all possible causes for the supposed independence of God's existence from essences, then demonstrates each leads to absurdity. Ready yourself for a rigorous logical exercise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Start by grasping the premise: God's existence is abstracted from being an accident to any essence. The proof examines what could cause this independence.
The premise is that God's existence is not an accident.
2
Identify the four divisions
The author lists four possible determinants of this independence. Familiarize yourself with each division: (1) existence itself, (2) an accident in existence, (3) a locus for existence, (4) a separate cause.
These divisions exhaust the logical possibilities.
3
Examine each refutation
Work through the refutation of each division. Notice the reasoning: the first leads to universal abstraction, the second to regress or contingency, the third to self-contradiction, the fourth to dependence on another.
Each refutation is a distinct argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof concludes that since all four divisions are false, the original claim (that God's existence is an accident) is false. Reflect on the overall logical structure.
The proof is a classic reductio ad absurdum by cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : وهي أيضا قريبة مما تقدم : إذا قلنا : إن وجود واجب الوجود مجردا عن كونه عارضا بشيء من الماهيات ، فالمقتضى لهذا التجرد ، ولهذا الاستغناء إما طبيعة الوجود من حيث إنه وجود وإما أن يكون حالا فيه ، وإما أن لا يكون حالا فيه ولا محلا له ، والكل باطل ، أما بيان بطلان القسم الأول فلأنه يلزم أن يكون كل وجود كذلك ، لأن لازم الماهية مشترك فيه بين جميع أفراد تلك الماهية ، وأما بيان بطلان القسم الثاني : فلأن تلك الصفة الحالة في ذلك الوجود ، إما أن تكون لازمة لذلك الوجود ، أو لا تكون لازمة له ، والأول باطل ، لأنه يعود إلى التقسيم في أن المقتضى لذلك اللزوم إما نفس طبيعة الوجود، وإما أن يكون حالا فيها أو عملا الهام أو ما لا يكون حالا فيها ولا محلا لها . فإن كان ذلك بسبب حال آخر . لزم التسلسل وهو محال ، وأما إن كانت تلك الصفة الحالة في ذلك الوجود ليست لازمة لذلك الوجود، فحينئذ يكون ممكن الزوال ، وبتقدير زواله فإنه يجب زوال ذلك الاستغناء ، ويجب زوال ذلك الاستقلال ، فحينئذ يصير الواجب لذاته ، ممكنا لذاته وهو محال . وأما بيان بطلان القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون المقتضى لذلك التجرد ، ولذلك الاستغناء شيء يكون محلا لذلك الوجود ، فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الوجود عارضا لذلك الشيء ، وتكون ماهية ذلك الشيء موصوفة بالوجود ، فحينئذ يكون وجود الله تعالى صفة قائمة بماهيته ، مع أن الفرض والتقدير هو أن وجود الله تعالى ليس عارضا لشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف . وأما بيان بطلان القسم الرابع : وهو أن كون ذلك الوجود مستقلا بنفسه، قائما بذاته، معلل بسبب منفصل، مغاير لذلك الوجود ، ولا يكون حالا فيه، ولا يكون محلا له . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لغيره ، وذلك محال . لأن بتقدير فرض عدم ذلك الغير إن لم يبق ذلك الشيء ، قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإن بقي قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لغيره ، ويثبت أن هذا الاجتماع باطل، فوجب القطع بأن هذا القسم محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان وجود الله تعالى وجودا قائما بنفسه ، لكان الحق هو أحد هذه الأقسام الأربعة ويثبت أن كل واحد منها باطل ، فيثبت أن ذلك الكلام باطل
The third proof: It is also close to what preceded: If we say that the existence of the Necessary Existent is abstracted from being an accident to any of the essences, then the determinant of this abstraction and this independence is either the nature of existence insofar as it is existence, or it is an accident in it, or it is neither an accident in it nor a locus for it, and all are false. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the first division: because it would necessitate that every existence is like that, for the necessary concomitant of the essence is shared among all individuals of that essence. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the second division: because that attribute inhering in that existence is either necessary for that existence or not necessary for it. The first is false, because it returns to the division: that the determinant of that necessity is either the nature of existence itself, or it is an accident in it or a locus for it, or it is neither an accident in it nor a locus for it. If that is due to another accident, then infinite regress follows, which is impossible. And if that attribute inhering in that existence is not necessary for that existence, then it would be possible of removal, and assuming its removal, then that independence would necessarily be removed, and that self-sufficiency would necessarily be removed; then the necessary by itself would become possible by itself, which is impossible. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the third division: that the determinant of that abstraction and that independence is something that is a locus for that existence, we say: This is false. Because on this assumption, existence would be an accident to that thing, and the essence of that thing would be described by existence; then the existence of Allah would be an attribute subsisting in His essence, whereas the hypothesis and assumption is that the existence of Allah is not an accident to any essence. This is a contradiction. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the fourth division: that this existence is independent in itself, self-subsisting, caused by a separate cause, different from that existence, and is neither an accident in it nor a locus for it. We say: On this assumption, it would necessarily follow that the Necessary Existent by itself is Necessary Existent by another, and that is impossible. Because assuming the non-existence of that other, if that thing does not remain, that would detract from its being necessary by itself; and if it remains, that would detract from its being necessary by another. And it is established that this combination is false, so it is necessary to conclude that this division is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that if the existence of Allah were an existence subsisting by itself, the truth would be one of these four divisions, and it is established that each of them is false, so it is established that that statement is false.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) that refutes the claim that God's existence is an accident added to His essence. The author argues by considering the implications of saying that God's existence is abstracted from being an accident. He divides the possible causes of this abstraction into four divisions: (1) it is due to the nature of existence itself, (2) due to an accident inhering in existence, (3) due to something that is a locus for existence, (4) due to a separate cause that is neither an accident nor a locus. He then refutes each division. The first would make all existences abstract, which is false. The second leads to either an infinite regress or the possibility of God's independence being removed, making the necessary contingent. The third makes God's existence an attribute of His essence, contradicting the assumption. The fourth makes God necessary by another, which is impossible. Therefore, the original claim is false.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (Wajib al-Wujud)
وجود (wujud)
عارض (arad)
ماهية (mahiyya)
استغناء (istighna')
استقلال (istiqlal)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
لازم (lazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's existence is a property like other properties, added to His essence.
Correction: This proof shows that any attempt to treat God's existence as an accident leads to logical contradictions, so it must be something entirely different.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a consequence of the second division in the third proof?
A) Infinite regress
B) That God's existence becomes an accident
C) That God's essence becomes an accident
D) That the nature of existence is shared

Key Takeaway
The third proof refutes the claim that God's existence is an accident by showing that all possible explanations for its independence lead to absurdity, thus establishing that God's existence is not an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if God's existence were an accident, it would lead to contradictions that make God dependent or contingent. Where in your own life do you still treat God's existence as if it is just one property among others, rather than the very ground of all reality? Will you today pause and acknowledge that He is absolutely independent—and that your own existence is utterly dependent on Him?
?
The fourth division reveals that being necessary by oneself and simultaneously dependent on another is impossible. Is there any area of your worship where you are trusting in something else alongside God—a habit, a person, your own efforts—as if it were necessary for your spiritual survival? What would it take to cut that false support and rely solely on the Self-Sufficient?
?
The argument uses rigorous logic to guard against reducing God's existence to an attribute. Have you ever thought of God's attributes as 'things' added to Him, rather than understanding that His existence is not like created existences? Will you commit to studying His names and attributes with the same intellectual seriousness as this proof demands, so that your knowledge leads to deeper awe?

Distinguishing Existence from Necessity in God
Proving the distinction between existence and necessity in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that distinguishes God's existence from His necessary self-existence.

Core Idea
Existence and necessity are not identical; they are conceptually distinct.

The fourth proof begins by recalling that the term 'existent' applies to both God and creatures. It then argues that if existence and necessity were identical, necessity's exclusivity would force existence to be exclusive too, which contradicts the fact that existence is shared. Hence they must be distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, options, refutation of the first option, conclusion.
The argument uses the fact that 'existent' is a univocal term.
2
Identify the two options presented
The author presents two possibilities: either the concepts of existence and necessity are identical or they are different. Mark these in the text.
3
Understand why the first option is false
The author argues that if they were identical, then since necessity is exclusive to God, existence would also be exclusive. But we know existence is shared with possibles, so the first option cannot hold.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, existence and necessity are distinct concepts. This is a key step for later analysis of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا قد دللنا على أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب وعلى الممكن ، بحسب مفهوم واحد . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن يكون المعقول من كونه تعالى موجودا ، والمعقول من كونه تعالى واجبا لذاته : أمر واحد من غير تغاير البتة ، وإما أن يكون المعقول من كل واحد منهما مغايرا للمعقول من الآخر. والأول باطل ، لأنه لما كان كونه واجبا لذاته غير مشترك فيه وبينه وبين الممكنات ، وجب أن لا يكون كونه موجودا أمرا مشتركا فيه بينه وبين الممكنات والوجوب الذاتي غير مشترك فيه بينه وبين الممكنات ، فوجب القطع بأن كونه موجودا مغايرا لكونه واجبا ، وإذا ثبت هذا التغاير فنقول : إما أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات إما أن يستلزم الوجود أو يقال بالعكس منه وهو أن الوجود يستلزم الوجوب بالذات ، أو يقال لا هذا يستلزم ذاك ولا بالعكس ، ولكن حصلت ماهية ثالثة . وتلك الماهية تكون مستلزمة لهذين القيدين
The fourth proof: We have already demonstrated that the term 'existent' applies to the Necessary and the Possible according to a single concept. And when this is established, we say: Either the conceptualization of His being existent and the conceptualization of His being necessary by Himself are one thing with no difference whatsoever, or the conceptualization of each of them is different from the conceptualization of the other. The first is false, because since His being necessary by Himself is not shared between Him and the possibles, it must be that His being existent is not a matter shared between Him and the possibles, and the self-necessity is not shared between Him and the possibles. So it is necessary to conclude that His being existent is different from His being necessary. And when this difference is established, we say: Either it is said that necessity by essence entails existence, or the opposite is said, that existence entails necessity by essence, or it is said that neither entails the other, but rather a third essence occurs, and that essence is the one that entails these two qualifications.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a) that distinguishes between existence (wujud) and necessary existence (wujub dhati) in God, and then examines the relationship between them. The author starts by recalling that 'existent' is a term that applies to both the Necessary and the possible by a single concept. Then he posits two options: either 'being existent' and 'being necessary by Himself' are identical, or they are different. The first is false because if they were identical, then the attribute of necessity (which is exclusive to God) would imply that existence is also exclusive, but we know that existence is shared with possibles. Therefore, they must be distinct. After establishing the distinction, he presents three possible relationships: necessity entails existence, existence entails necessity, or neither entails the other but a third essence entails both.

Key Terms
موجود (mawjud)
واجب (wajib)
ممكن (mumkin)
تغاير (taghayur)
مفهوم (mafhum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence and necessity are the same attribute in God.
Correction: This proof shows they are conceptually distinct; existence is shared with creatures while necessity is not.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise that the fourth proof relies on before presenting the dichotomy?
A) That the term 'existent' applies only to God
B) That the term 'existent' applies univocally to both God and possibles
C) That necessity and existence are identical
D) That possibles do not exist

Key Takeaway
Existence and necessary self-existence are distinct concepts; the former is shared, the latter is exclusive to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you sometimes reduce His reality to just 'existence' without recognizing the unique necessity that sets Him apart from all else? How would your worship change if you constantly remembered that His necessity is exclusive to Him?
?
If you truly believed that God's existence is shared with every creature, would you still feel the same awe and reverence? Does the distinction this lesson makes help you appreciate His uniqueness more? Commit to reflecting on this difference daily for a week.
?
This lesson shows that even the concept of existence is common between God and creation. Does that humble you, knowing that your very existence is a gift from Him? How can you use this awareness to turn away from self-sufficiency and rely on Him alone?

Three Views on the Relationship Between Existence and Necessity
Enumerating three possible relationships between existence and necessity after their distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible relationships between existence and necessity in God after their distinction.

Core Idea
After distinguishing existence and necessity, three logical possibilities about their relation emerge.

Having established that existence and necessity are distinct, the author now examines how they relate. Three options are presented: necessity entails existence, existence entails necessity, or neither entails the other and a third essence entails both. This lesson unpacks these possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that after establishing the distinction, the author lists three options for the relationship between existence and necessity.
The passage ends without settling which option is correct; it just presents them.
2
Identify the three options
List them: (1) necessity entails existence, (2) existence entails necessity, (3) neither entails the other but a third essence entails both.
3
Consider the logical implications
Reflect on what each option would mean for divine simplicity and the relationship between attributes. The author does not choose one here.
4
Connect to broader theology
These options lay the groundwork for discussions on whether God's attributes are identical with His essence or not.

Refutation of Existence as an Accident to Quiddity
Refutation of the third division: existence as accident to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's existence is an accident to His quiddity leads to a contradiction, and thus cannot be true.

Core Idea
God's existence cannot be an accident to His essence—that would make Him dependent on His own essence.

This passage presents a concise refutation of the idea that existence is an accidental attribute added to the quiddity. The argument shows that if existence were an accident, then God's existence would be an attribute subsisting in His essence, which contradicts the fundamental premise that God's existence is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a direct application of logical contradiction (خلف) in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The author assumes a classification where existence could be an accident to the quiddity. Grasp what this means: existence is not essential but added on.
2
Follow the reductio
See how the author deduces that if existence is an accident, then God's existence becomes an attribute of His quiddity, directly contradicting the assumption that it is not an accident.
3
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion 'this is a contradiction' (خلف) ends the refutation. This is a standard argument form in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود ، فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الوجود عارضا لذلك الشيء ، وتكون ماهية ذلك الشيء موصوفة بالوجود ، فحينئذ يكون وجود الله تعالى صفة قائمة بماهيته ، مع أن الفرض والتقدير هو أن وجود الله تعالى ليس عارضا لشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف .
وأما بيان بطلان القسم الرابع : وهو أن كون ذلك الوجود مستقلا بنفسه، قائما بذاته، معلل بسبب منفصل، مغاير لذلك الوجود ، ولا يكون حالا فيه، ولا يكون محلا له . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لغيره ، وذلك محال . لأن بتقدير فرض عدم ذلك الغير إن لم يبق ذلك الشيء ، قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإن بقي قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لغيره ، ويثبت أن هذا الاجتماع باطل، فوجب القطع بأن هذا القسم محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان وجود الله تعالى وجودا قائما بنفسه ، لكان الحق هو أحد هذه الأقسام الأربعة ويثبت أن كل واحد منها باطل ، فيثبت أن ذلك الكلام باطل
Existence, so we say: this is false. Because on this assumption, existence would be an accident to that thing, and the quiddity of that thing would be described by existence, so then the existence of God would be an attribute subsisting in His quiddity, while the hypothesis and assumption is that the existence of God is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a contradiction.
As for the exposition of the falsity of the fourth division: which is that that existence is independent in itself, subsisting by itself, caused by a separate cause, different from that existence, and it is not a state in it, nor is it a locus for it. We say: on this assumption, it would necessitate that the necessary existent by itself is necessary existent by another, and that is impossible. Because if we suppose the non-existence of that other, if that thing does not remain, that impugns its being necessary existent by itself; and if it remains, that impugns its being necessary existent by another; and it is established that this combination is false, so one must necessarily judge that this division is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that if the existence of God were an existence subsisting by itself, the correct would be one of these four divisions, and it is established that each one of them is false, so it is established that that statement is false.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage refutes two remaining divisions in a classification about God's existence being self-subsistent. The author begins by refuting the third division (implied by the context): that existence is an accident to the quiddity. He argues that if existence were an accident, then God's existence would be an attribute subsisting in His quiddity, contradicting the premise that God's existence is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a contradiction (خلف). Then he turns to the fourth division: that the existence is independent, self-subsistent, but caused by a separate cause. This leads to a contradiction: it would make God necessary by itself and necessary by another simultaneously, which is impossible. He explains that if the separate cause is supposed nonexistent, then either the thing remains (implying it is necessary by itself) or it does not remain (implying it depends on the cause). Both options impugn the claim of being necessary by itself or by another. Therefore, this division is impossible. The conclusion is that all four divisions of the claim are false, so the original statement that God's existence is self-subsistent is false.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
عارض
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that describing existence as an accident to the quiddity is a plausible way to talk about God's existence.
Correction: The argument shows that this leads to a contradiction because it makes God's existence an attribute of His essence, which violates the premise that it is not an accident.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author derive from the third division?
A) It makes God's existence an attribute of His quiddity, contrary to the premise that it is not an accident.
B) It makes God's existence dependent on another cause.
C) It makes God's existence identical to His quiddity.
D) It makes God's existence a separate substance.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God's existence is an accident to His quiddity is impossible because it makes His existence an attribute of His essence, contradicting the premise that it is not an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence were an accident to His essence, then His existence would depend on His essence. Do you ever treat God's existence as something added on to your life rather than the very ground of your being? How would your daily mindset change if you truly believed that God's existence is not accidental but essential to everything?
?
The refutation shows that assuming existence as an accident leads to a contradiction. In your own life, are there beliefs about God that you hold that are self-contradictory? Where might you be treating God's attributes as separate from His essence, and what would it mean to unify them in your understanding?
?
This passage guards against thinking of God's existence as a mere attribute. Do you sometimes reduce God to a 'part' of your worldview rather than the foundation? What specific habit can you adopt today that reflects that God is not an accident in your life but the very reason you exist?

Refutation of Self-Subsistent Existence Caused by Another
Refutation of the fourth division and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the idea of a self-subsistent existence that is nonetheless caused by a separate cause is impossible, as it leads to a contradiction in the concept of necessary existence.

Core Idea
Nothing can be necessary by itself and necessary by another at the same time—that would be a contradiction.

This passage addresses the fourth division of a classification about God's existence, where the existence is independent and self-subsistent but caused by a separate cause. The author argues that this would make God both necessary by itself (واجب الوجود لذاته) and necessary by another (واجب الوجود لغيره), which is impossible. He then uses a hypothetical test of non-existence of the cause to show the contradiction, and concludes that this division is impossible, thereby invalidating the entire classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourth division
The division states that existence is self-subsistent but caused by a separate cause. Note that this implies both independence and dependence.
2
Identify the contradiction
The author points out that this would make the entity both necessary by itself and necessary by another, which is impossible.
3
Test with non-existence of the cause
The argument proceeds by supposing the cause does not exist. If the entity remains, it shows it is not truly dependent on the cause (so not necessary by another). If it ceases, it shows it is not necessary by itself. Both options impugn the claim.
4
Generalize the conclusion
The author concludes that all four divisions are false, so the original statement that God's existence is self-subsistent is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود ، فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الوجود عارضا لذلك الشيء ، وتكون ماهية ذلك الشيء موصوفة بالوجود ، فحينئذ يكون وجود الله تعالى صفة قائمة بماهيته ، مع أن الفرض والتقدير هو أن وجود الله تعالى ليس عارضا لشيء من الماهيات . هذا خلف .
وأما بيان بطلان القسم الرابع : وهو أن كون ذلك الوجود مستقلا بنفسه، قائما بذاته، معلل بسبب منفصل، مغاير لذلك الوجود ، ولا يكون حالا فيه، ولا يكون محلا له . فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، واجب الوجود لغيره ، وذلك محال . لأن بتقدير فرض عدم ذلك الغير إن لم يبق ذلك الشيء ، قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لذاته ، وإن بقي قدح ذلك في كونه واجب الوجود لغيره ، ويثبت أن هذا الاجتماع باطل، فوجب القطع بأن هذا القسم محال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان وجود الله تعالى وجودا قائما بنفسه ، لكان الحق هو أحد هذه الأقسام الأربعة ويثبت أن كل واحد منها باطل ، فيثبت أن ذلك الكلام باطل
Existence, so we say: this is false. Because on this assumption, existence would be an accident to that thing, and the quiddity of that thing would be described by existence, so then the existence of God would be an attribute subsisting in His quiddity, while the hypothesis and assumption is that the existence of God is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a contradiction.
As for the exposition of the falsity of the fourth division: which is that that existence is independent in itself, subsisting by itself, caused by a separate cause, different from that existence, and it is not a state in it, nor is it a locus for it. We say: on this assumption, it would necessitate that the necessary existent by itself is necessary existent by another, and that is impossible. Because if we suppose the non-existence of that other, if that thing does not remain, that impugns its being necessary existent by itself; and if it remains, that impugns its being necessary existent by another; and it is established that this combination is false, so one must necessarily judge that this division is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that if the existence of God were an existence subsisting by itself, the correct would be one of these four divisions, and it is established that each one of them is false, so it is established that that statement is false.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage refutes two remaining divisions in a classification about God's existence being self-subsistent. The author begins by refuting the third division (implied by the context): that existence is an accident to the quiddity. He argues that if existence were an accident, then God's existence would be an attribute subsisting in His quiddity, contradicting the premise that God's existence is not an accident to any quiddity. This is a contradiction (خلف). Then he turns to the fourth division: that the existence is independent, self-subsistent, but caused by a separate cause. This leads to a contradiction: it would make God necessary by itself and necessary by another simultaneously, which is impossible. He explains that if the separate cause is supposed nonexistent, then either the thing remains (implying it is necessary by itself) or it does not remain (implying it depends on the cause). Both options impugn the claim of being necessary by itself or by another. Therefore, this division is impossible. The conclusion is that all four divisions of the claim are false, so the original statement that God's existence is self-subsistent is false.

واجب الوجود لغيره
معلل
حال
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that something can be both self-sufficient and caused, as long as the cause is external.
Correction: The argument shows that the two are mutually exclusive because the entity's necessity either depends on itself or on another, and the hypothetical test demonstrates the contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the hypothetical non-existence of the separate cause?
A) The entity remains, so it is necessary by another.
B) The entity ceases, so it is necessary by itself.
C) Both options show impossibility, so the fourth division is false.
D) The entity remains, proving it is not caused.

Key Takeaway
The fourth division fails because it requires an entity to be both necessary by itself and necessary by another, which is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that nothing can be both self-sufficient and caused by another, how would you stop trying to earn God's favor through your own deeds as if they were independently valuable?
?
Consider something you rely on deeply—your health, wealth, or relationships. If you were to suppose that they could be taken away at any moment, would your faith in God remain? Or does your peace depend on those things remaining? This lesson shows that nothing caused by another can be self-sufficient. Your existence is entirely caused by God. Does your heart accept that you have no independent standing before Him, or do you subtly believe you can 'be good enough' on your own? Let this truth expose any hidden self-reliance.
