The Core Disjunction: Self-Subsistent or Dependent
Sanusi introduces Ibn Sina's proof with a disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial disjunction in Ibn Sina's proof: God's existence is either self-subsistent and independent, or it is dependent on something else.

Core Idea
God's existence is either self-sufficient or requires a cause—no middle ground.

Ibn Sina begins his proof with a clear either/or: God's existence must be either independent or not. This binary sets the stage for the entire argument. If it is independent, then it cannot be an accident of any essence. If it is dependent, then we must trace its cause. Let's examine this first branch.

Guided Path
1
Read the disjunctive premise
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents Ibn Sina's argument. Notice the clear 'إما... وإما' (either...or) structure.
This is a classic logical disjunction.
2
Understand the first option
The first possibility is that God's existence is 'مستقل بنفسه' (independent in itself). Identify why this would mean it cannot be an accident.
Accidents depend on a subject; a self-subsistent existence cannot be accidental.
3
Pause before the second option
The second option (dependent) is introduced but not yet analyzed. Recognize that the rest of the proof will handle this branch.
The argument splits here; the next lessons cover the dependent branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي في بيان أن وجود الله تعالى يمتنع كونه عارضا لشيء من الماهيات بوجه واحد ذكره في كتاب الإشارات. وأنا أذكر ذلك الوجه، وأضم إليه تقريرات قوية، وبيانات كاملة. فنقول: وجود الله تعالى إما أن يكون مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته، من غير أن يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره. فإن كان الحق هو الأول، كان ذلك الوجود قائما بذاته مستقلا بنفسه، فلا يكون عارضا لشيء من الماهيات. وإن كان الحق هو الثاني، وهو كون ذلك الوجود غير مستقل بنفسه بل يكون مفتقرا إلى غيره، فنقول: فذلك الوجود مفتقر إلى غيره، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فذلك الوجود ممكن لذاته. وكل ممكن لذاته فلا بد له من سبب، وذلك السبب إما تلك الماهية أو غيرها. ولا جائز أن يكون سبب ذلك الوجود هو تلك الماهية، لأن كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لذلك الوجود، إما أن يكون مشروطا بكونها موجودة أو لا يكون مشروطا بهذا الشرط. ولا جائز أن يكون اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروطا بكونها موجودة، لأن الوجود الذي هو شرط الاقتضاء، إن كان هو الوجود الذي هو الأثر لزم كون الشيء الواحد شرطا لنفسه، وهو محال، وإن كان غيره لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين، وهو محال. ولأن الكلام في الوجود الثاني كالكلام في الأول، يلزم إما التسلسل، وإما الدور وهما محالان. هذا كله إذا قلنا: إن اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروط بكون تلك الماهية موجودة. أما إن قلنا: إن هذا الشرط غير معتبر، فحينئذ يكون هذا قولا بأن ما ليس بموجود، يكون مقتضيا للوجود، وذلك محال في بديهة العقل، لأن الشيء ما لم يكن موجودا في نفسه، يمتنع كونه علة في وجود غيره. وهذا كله إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود هي تلك الماهية. أما إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود شيء آخر سوى تلك الماهية، فحينئذ يكون وجود واجب الوجود لذاته، مستفادا من سبب منفصل، فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود بغيره، وذلك محال. فثبت أن القول بأن وجود واجب الوجود لذاته عارض لماهيته: يفضي إلى هذه المحالات، فوجب أن يكون القول به محالا. فثبت أنه لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد عن جميع الماهيات. وهذا هو تقرير هذه الحجة على أقوى الوجوه.
And the Chief Master Abu Ali argued in explaining that the existence of God, exalted, is impossible to be an accident of any quiddity, by a single argument he mentioned in the Book of Pointers. And I will mention that argument and append to it strong expositions and complete explanations. So we say: The existence of God, exalted, is either independent in itself, subsisting by itself, without its reality needing the realization of something else, or it is not like that, but rather its reality needs the realization of something else. If the truth is the first, then that existence is subsisting by itself, independent in itself, so it is not an accident of any quiddity. If the truth is the second—i.e., that existence is not independent in itself but rather is in need of another—then we say: That existence is in need of another, and everything in need of another is possible in itself, so that existence is possible in itself. And everything possible in itself necessarily requires a cause, and that cause is either that quiddity or something else. And it is not possible that the cause of that existence is that quiddity, because the fact that that quiddity necessitates that existence either depends on its being existent or does not depend on this condition. And it is not possible that the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon its being existent, because if the existence which is the condition of necessitation is the same as the existence which is the effect, then it would entail that one thing is a condition for itself, which is impossible. And if it is different, then it would entail that one thing exists twice, which is impossible. And because the discussion about the second existence is like the discussion about the first, it would entail either infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. All this is if we say: the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon that quiddity being existent. But if we say: this condition is not considered, then this would be a statement that what is not existent can necessitate existence, and that is impossible in the intuition of the intellect, because a thing, as long as it is not existent in itself, cannot be a cause for the existence of another. And all this is if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is that quiddity. But if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is something other than that quiddity, then the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself would be derived from a separate cause, thus the Necessary Existent in itself would be necessary by another, and that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself is an accident of His quiddity leads to these impossibilities, so it must be impossible to hold it. So it is established that He has no reality except existence abstracted from all quiddities. And this is the exposition of this argument in the strongest manner.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Avicenna (Ibn Sina) against the view that God's existence is an accidental attribute of His essence, presented and strengthened by the author. The argument proceeds by a disjunction: God's existence is either self-subsistent and independent, or not. If it is self-subsistent, then it cannot be an accident (since accidents need a subject). If it is dependent, then it is possible in itself and needs a cause. That cause could be the quiddity itself or something else. If the quiddity is the cause, the author examines two possibilities: either the quiddity's causation is conditioned on its own existence or not. If conditioned, then either the condition is identical with the effect (leading to self-causing, impossible) or different (leading to double existence, also impossible). If not conditioned, then a non-existent thing would be causing existence, which is intuitively absurd. If the cause is something other than the quiddity, then God's existence would be derived from another, making Him necessary by another (not by Himself), which contradicts the definition of the Necessary Existent. Therefore, all options lead to impossibility, proving that God's existence cannot be an accident; rather, His reality is pure existence free from quiddities. The author concludes by affirming this as the strongest formulation of the argument.

Key Terms
الشيخ الرئيس
وجود الله
عارض
ماهية
مستقل بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the disjunction presented, if God's existence is independent, what does it mean?
A) It can be an accident of something else.
B) It is self-subsistent and cannot be an accident.
C) It is dependent on a quiddity.
D) It is possible in itself.

Key Takeaway
The first branch of the disjunction shows that if God's existence is self-subsistent, it cannot be an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's existence is independent and self-subsistent, needing nothing. But you often act as if your own existence, your job, your relationships, or your health are independent. When was the last moment today you felt anxious because you forgot that only God is truly self-sufficient? Will you pause right now and say, 'I depend on Him alone'?
?
If God's existence cannot be an accident, then it is essential. Your existence is contingent—a gift from Him. Does your daily life reflect that you are a dependent being? Name one thing you treat as if it is essential to your happiness that is actually an accident—something you could lose. How will you reattach your heart to the only Self-Subsistent One?
?
The argument uses a basic logical disjunction. Have you ever honestly faced the disjunction in your own belief: either God is independent or not? If you truly believe He is independent, does your reliance on His creation mirror that belief? Commit today to one concrete action that shows your total dependence on Him.

The Dependent Branch: Impossible Consequences
Sanusi analyzes the dependent branch, showing it leads to impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if God's existence is dependent, it would lead to a chain of impossibilities such as self-causation, infinite regress, or a non-existent cause.

Core Idea
A dependent existence leads to contradictions, proving it cannot be.

Having rejected the independent branch as leading to the conclusion that existence is not an accident, Ibn Sina now examines the other possibility: that existence is dependent. He argues that this leads to absurdities. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Trace the dependent hypothesis
Read the passage from 'وإن كان الحق هو الثاني' to 'وهذا كله إذا قلنا العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود هي تلك الماهية'. Notice how the author derives that dependent existence is possible in itself and needs a cause.
The key term is 'ممكن لذاته' (possible in itself).
2
Analyze the two options for the cause
The cause is either the quiddity itself or something else. The passage first examines the quiddity as cause.
This is a crucial fork in the argument.
3
Understand the conditional analysis
If the quiddity is the cause, its causation is either conditioned on its own existence or not. Both lead to impossibilities (self-causation, double existence, non-existent cause).
These are classic arguments against self-causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the Chief Master Abu Ali argued in explaining that the existence of God, exalted, is impossible to be an accident of any quiddity, by a single argument he mentioned in the Book of Pointers. And I will mention that argument and append to it strong expositions and complete explanations. So we say: The existence of God, exalted, is either independent in itself, subsisting by itself, without its reality needing the realization of something else, or it is not like that, but rather its reality needs the realization of something else. If the truth is the first, then that existence is subsisting by itself, independent in itself, so it is not an accident of any quiddity. Commentary
This passage is a proof by Avicenna (Ibn Sina) against the view that God's existence is an accidental attribute of His essence, presented and strengthened by the author. ?

The Alternative Cause and Final Conclusion
Sanusi completes the argument by refuting the alternative cause and affirming God as pure existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if God's existence is dependent, the alternative cause (something other than the quiddity) leads to making God necessary by another, which is impossible, and therefore God's existence is pure existence without a quiddity.

Core Idea
God's existence cannot be an accident; He is pure existence.

After refuting the possibility that the quiddity is the cause of God's existence, Ibn Sina examines the only remaining possibility: that the cause is something other than the quiddity. This too leads to a contradiction, and the argument concludes that God's existence is not an accidental attribute but rather the reality of God is pure existence, abstracted from all quiddities. Let's read the final part.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative cause argument
Read from 'أما إذا قلنا العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود شيء آخر' to the end. Notice that if the cause is separate, then God's existence would be derived from another, making Him necessary by another (واجب الوجود بالغير), which contradicts His being necessary by Himself.
This is a direct contradiction to the definition of the Necessary Existent.
2
Understand the final conclusion
The argument concludes that all attempts to make existence an accident of a quiddity lead to impossibilities, so the only coherent position is that God has no quiddity other than His existence.
This is a strong metaphysical thesis.
3
Reflect on the strength of the argument
The author claims this is the strongest exposition of the proof. Consider how the disjunctive method systematically eliminates all alternatives.
This is a model of philosophical argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي في بيان أن وجود الله تعالى يمتنع كونه عارضا لشيء من الماهيات بوجه واحد ذكره في كتاب الإشارات. وأنا أذكر ذلك الوجه، وأضم إليه تقريرات قوية، وبيانات كاملة. فنقول: وجود الله تعالى إما أن يكون مستقلا بنفسه قائما بذاته، من غير أن يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون تحققه محتاجا إلى تحقق غيره. فإن كان الحق هو الأول، كان ذلك الوجود قائما بذاته مستقلا بنفسه، فلا يكون عارضا لشيء من الماهيات. وإن كان الحق هو الثاني، وهو كون ذلك الوجود غير مستقل بنفسه بل يكون مفتقرا إلى غيره، فنقول: فذلك الوجود مفتقر إلى غيره، وكل مفتقر إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته، فذلك الوجود ممكن لذاته. وكل ممكن لذاته فلا بد له من سبب، وذلك السبب إما تلك الماهية أو غيرها. ولا جائز أن يكون سبب ذلك الوجود هو تلك الماهية، لأن كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لذلك الوجود، إما أن يكون مشروطا بكونها موجودة أو لا يكون مشروطا بهذا الشرط. ولا جائز أن يكون اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروطا بكونها موجودة، لأن الوجود الذي هو شرط الاقتضاء، إن كان هو الوجود الذي هو الأثر لزم كون الشيء الواحد شرطا لنفسه، وهو محال، وإن كان غيره لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد موجودا مرتين، وهو محال. ولأن الكلام في الوجود الثاني كالكلام في الأول، يلزم إما التسلسل، وإما الدور وهما محالان. هذا كله إذا قلنا: إن اقتضاء تلك الماهية لذلك الوجود، مشروط بكون تلك الماهية موجودة. أما إن قلنا: إن هذا الشرط غير معتبر، فحينئذ يكون هذا قولا بأن ما ليس بموجود، يكون مقتضيا للوجود، وذلك محال في بديهة العقل، لأن الشيء ما لم يكن موجودا في نفسه، يمتنع كونه علة في وجود غيره. وهذا كله إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود هي تلك الماهية. أما إذا قلنا: العلة المقتضية لذلك الوجود شيء آخر سوى تلك الماهية، فحينئذ يكون وجود واجب الوجود لذاته، مستفادا من سبب منفصل، فيكون واجب الوجود لذاته واجب الوجود بغيره، وذلك محال. فثبت أن القول بأن وجود واجب الوجود لذاته عارض لماهيته: يفضي إلى هذه المحالات، فوجب أن يكون القول به محالا. فثبت أنه لا حقيقة له إلا الوجود المجرد عن جميع الماهيات. وهذا هو تقرير هذه الحجة على أقوى الوجوه.
And the Chief Master Abu Ali argued in explaining that the existence of God, exalted, is impossible to be an accident of any quiddity, by a single argument he mentioned in the Book of Pointers. And I will mention that argument and append to it strong expositions and complete explanations. So we say: The existence of God, exalted, is either independent in itself, subsisting by itself, without its reality needing the realization of something else, or it is not like that, but rather its reality needs the realization of something else. If the truth is the first, then that existence is subsisting by itself, independent in itself, so it is not an accident of any quiddity. If the truth is the second—i.e., that existence is not independent in itself but rather is in need of another—then we say: That existence is in need of another, and everything in need of another is possible in itself, so that existence is possible in itself. And everything possible in itself necessarily requires a cause, and that cause is either that quiddity or something else. And it is not possible that the cause of that existence is that quiddity, because the fact that that quiddity necessitates that existence either depends on its being existent or does not depend on this condition. And it is not possible that the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon its being existent, because if the existence which is the condition of necessitation is the same as the existence which is the effect, then it would entail that one thing is a condition for itself, which is impossible. And if it is different, then it would entail that one thing exists twice, which is impossible. And because the discussion about the second existence is like the discussion about the first, it would entail either infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. All this is if we say: the necessitation of that quiddity for that existence is conditioned upon that quiddity being existent. But if we say: this condition is not considered, then this would be a statement that what is not existent can necessitate existence, and that is impossible in the intuition of the intellect, because a thing, as long as it is not existent in itself, cannot be a cause for the existence of another. And all this is if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is that quiddity. But if we say: the necessitating cause of that existence is something other than that quiddity, then the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself would be derived from a separate cause, thus the Necessary Existent in itself would be necessary by another, and that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that the existence of the Necessary Existent in itself is an accident of His quiddity leads to these impossibilities, so it must be impossible to hold it. So it is established that He has no reality except existence abstracted from all quiddities. And this is the exposition of this argument in the strongest manner.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Avicenna (Ibn Sina) against the view that God's existence is an accidental attribute of His essence, presented and strengthened by the author. The argument proceeds by a disjunction: God's existence is either self-subsistent and independent, or not. If it is self-subsistent, then it cannot be an accident (since accidents need a subject). If it is dependent, then it is possible in itself and needs a cause. That cause could be the quiddity itself or something else. If the quiddity is the cause, the author examines two possibilities: either the quiddity's causation is conditioned on its own existence or not. If conditioned, then either the condition is identical with the effect (leading to self-causing, impossible) or different (leading to double existence, also impossible). If not conditioned, then a non-existent thing would be causing existence, which is intuitively absurd. If the cause is something other than the quiddity, then God's existence would be derived from another, making Him necessary by another (not by Himself), which contradicts the definition of the Necessary Existent. Therefore, all options lead to impossibility, proving that God's existence cannot be an accident; rather, His reality is pure existence free from quiddities. The author concludes by affirming this as the strongest formulation of the argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's quiddity and existence are distinct, but necessary by Himself.
Correction: The argument shows that if existence were accidental to a quiddity, it leads to contradictions; therefore, God's existence is not an accident, and He is pure existence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion drawn from the argument?
A) God's existence is an accident of His quiddity.
B) God has no quiddity, only pure existence.
C) God's existence is dependent on another.
D) The argument is inconclusive.

Key Takeaway
The proof concludes that God's existence is not an accident; He is pure existence, with no quiddity separate from His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument concludes that God has no quiddity; He is pure existence. If you truly believed that God's existence is not accidental but absolute, how would your reliance on created causes change? What would you start doing today to depend on Him alone?
?
Sanusi shows that if God's existence were caused by something else, He would be contingent. Are there things in your life that you treat as necessary causes for your well-being, as if God's existence depends on them? Identify one such thing and commit to detaching from it.
?
The proof claims God's reality is existence abstracted from quiddity. Does your prayer and worship reflect this absolute, uncaused reality, or do you still think of God as a being with a 'whatness' similar to creatures? What specific change in your worship would align with this truth?
